	<h1 id="firstHeading" class="firstHeading" lang="en">Charles I of England</h1>
	
	<div id="bodyContent" class="mw-body-content">
		<div id="siteSub" class="noprint">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>
		<div id="contentSub"></div>
		
		
		<div id="jump-to-nav"></div>
		<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#mw-head">Jump to navigation</a>
		<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#p-search">Jump to search</a>
		<div id="mw-content-text" lang="en" dir="ltr" class="mw-content-ltr"><div class="mw-parser-output"><p class="mw-empty-elt">


</p>
<div class="shortdescription nomobile noexcerpt noprint searchaux" style="display:none">17th-century monarch of kingdoms of England, Scotland, and Ireland</div>
<p class="mw-empty-elt">
</p>
<div class="shortdescription nomobile noexcerpt noprint searchaux" style="display:none">King of England and Ireland</div>

<p><b>Charles I</b> (19 November 1600&#160;– 30 January 1649)<sup id="cite_ref-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-1">&#91;a&#93;</a></sup> was <a href="/wiki/King_of_England" class="mw-redirect" title="King of England">King of England</a>, <a href="/wiki/King_of_Scotland" class="mw-redirect" title="King of Scotland">King of Scotland</a>, and <a href="/wiki/King_of_Ireland" class="mw-redirect" title="King of Ireland">King of Ireland</a> from 27 March 1625 until <a href="/wiki/Execution_of_Charles_I" title="Execution of Charles I">his execution</a> in 1649.
</p><p>Charles was born into the <a href="/wiki/House_of_Stuart" title="House of Stuart">House of Stuart</a> as the second son of <a href="/wiki/King_James_VI_of_Scotland" class="mw-redirect" title="King James VI of Scotland">King James VI of Scotland</a>, but after his father inherited the English throne in 1603 (as James I), he moved to England, where he spent much of the rest of his life. He became <a href="/wiki/Heir_apparent" title="Heir apparent">heir apparent</a> to the three kingdoms of <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_England" title="Kingdom of England">England</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Scotland" title="Kingdom of Scotland">Scotland</a> and <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Ireland" title="Kingdom of Ireland">Ireland</a> in 1612 on the death of his elder brother <a href="/wiki/Henry_Frederick,_Prince_of_Wales" title="Henry Frederick, Prince of Wales">Henry Frederick, Prince of Wales</a>. An unsuccessful and unpopular attempt to marry him to the <a href="/wiki/Spanish_Habsburg" class="mw-redirect" title="Spanish Habsburg">Spanish Habsburg</a> princess <a href="/wiki/Maria_Anna_of_Spain" title="Maria Anna of Spain">Maria Anna</a> culminated in an eight-month visit to Spain in 1623 that demonstrated the futility of the marriage negotiations. Two years later he married the <a href="/wiki/House_of_Bourbon" title="House of Bourbon">Bourbon</a> princess <a href="/wiki/Henrietta_Maria_of_France" title="Henrietta Maria of France">Henrietta Maria of France</a>.
</p><p>After his succession in 1625, Charles quarrelled with the <a href="/wiki/Parliament_of_England" title="Parliament of England">Parliament of England</a>, which sought to curb his <a href="/wiki/Royal_prerogative" title="Royal prerogative">royal prerogative</a>. Charles believed in the <a href="/wiki/Divine_right_of_kings" title="Divine right of kings">divine right of kings</a>, and was determined to govern according to his own conscience. Many of his subjects opposed his policies, in particular the levying of taxes without parliamentary consent, and perceived his actions as those of a tyrannical <a href="/wiki/Absolute_monarch" class="mw-redirect" title="Absolute monarch">absolute monarch</a>. His religious policies, coupled with his marriage to a <a href="/wiki/Roman_Catholic" class="mw-redirect" title="Roman Catholic">Roman Catholic</a>, generated the antipathy and mistrust of <a href="/wiki/Reformed" class="mw-redirect" title="Reformed">Reformed</a> groups such as the English <a href="/wiki/Puritan" class="mw-redirect" title="Puritan">Puritans</a> and Scottish <a href="/wiki/Covenanter" class="mw-redirect" title="Covenanter">Covenanters</a>, who thought his views were too Catholic. He supported <a href="/wiki/High_church" title="High church">high church</a> <a href="/wiki/Anglican" class="mw-redirect" title="Anglican">Anglican</a> ecclesiastics, such as <a href="/wiki/Richard_Montagu" title="Richard Montagu">Richard Montagu</a> and <a href="/wiki/William_Laud" title="William Laud">William Laud</a>, and failed to aid <a href="/wiki/Protestant" class="mw-redirect" title="Protestant">Protestant</a> forces successfully during the <a href="/wiki/Thirty_Years%27_War" title="Thirty Years&#39; War">Thirty Years' War</a>. His attempts to force the <a href="/wiki/Church_of_Scotland" title="Church of Scotland">Church of Scotland</a> to adopt high Anglican practices led to the <a href="/wiki/Bishops%27_Wars" title="Bishops&#39; Wars">Bishops' Wars</a>, strengthened the position of the English and Scottish parliaments, and helped precipitate his own downfall.
</p><p>From 1642, Charles fought the armies of the English and Scottish parliaments in the <a href="/wiki/English_Civil_War" title="English Civil War">English Civil War</a>. After his defeat in 1645, he surrendered to a Scottish force that eventually handed him over to the English Parliament. Charles refused to accept his captors' demands for a <a href="/wiki/Constitutional_monarchy" title="Constitutional monarchy">constitutional monarchy</a>, and temporarily escaped captivity in November 1647. Re-imprisoned on the <a href="/wiki/Isle_of_Wight" title="Isle of Wight">Isle of Wight</a>, Charles forged an alliance with Scotland, but by the end of 1648 <a href="/wiki/Oliver_Cromwell" title="Oliver Cromwell">Oliver Cromwell</a>'s <a href="/wiki/New_Model_Army" title="New Model Army">New Model Army</a> had consolidated its control over England. Charles was <a href="/wiki/High_Court_of_Justice_for_the_trial_of_King_Charles_I" class="mw-redirect" title="High Court of Justice for the trial of King Charles I">tried</a>, convicted, and <a href="/wiki/Execution_of_Charles_I" title="Execution of Charles I">executed</a> for <a href="/wiki/High_treason" class="mw-redirect" title="High treason">high treason</a> in January 1649. The monarchy was abolished and the <a href="/wiki/Commonwealth_of_England" title="Commonwealth of England">Commonwealth of England</a> was established as a <a href="/wiki/Republic" title="Republic">republic</a>. The monarchy would be <a href="/wiki/English_Restoration" class="mw-redirect" title="English Restoration">restored</a> to Charles's son, <a href="/wiki/Charles_II_of_England" title="Charles II of England">Charles II</a>, in 1660.
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_life">Early life</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Anne_of_Denmark;_King_Charles_I_when_Prince_of_Wales;_King_James_I_of_England_and_VI_of_Scotland_by_Simon_De_Passe_(2).jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fa/Anne_of_Denmark%3B_King_Charles_I_when_Prince_of_Wales%3B_King_James_I_of_England_and_VI_of_Scotland_by_Simon_De_Passe_%282%29.jpg/170px-Anne_of_Denmark%3B_King_Charles_I_when_Prince_of_Wales%3B_King_James_I_of_England_and_VI_of_Scotland_by_Simon_De_Passe_%282%29.jpg" decoding="async" width="170" height="208" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fa/Anne_of_Denmark%3B_King_Charles_I_when_Prince_of_Wales%3B_King_James_I_of_England_and_VI_of_Scotland_by_Simon_De_Passe_%282%29.jpg/255px-Anne_of_Denmark%3B_King_Charles_I_when_Prince_of_Wales%3B_King_James_I_of_England_and_VI_of_Scotland_by_Simon_De_Passe_%282%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fa/Anne_of_Denmark%3B_King_Charles_I_when_Prince_of_Wales%3B_King_James_I_of_England_and_VI_of_Scotland_by_Simon_De_Passe_%282%29.jpg/340px-Anne_of_Denmark%3B_King_Charles_I_when_Prince_of_Wales%3B_King_James_I_of_England_and_VI_of_Scotland_by_Simon_De_Passe_%282%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2400" data-file-height="2942" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Anne_of_Denmark;_King_Charles_I_when_Prince_of_Wales;_King_James_I_of_England_and_VI_of_Scotland_by_Simon_De_Passe_(2).jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Engraving by <a href="/wiki/Simon_de_Passe" class="mw-redirect" title="Simon de Passe">Simon de Passe</a> of Charles and his parents, King James and Queen Anne, c. 1612</div></div></div>
<p>The second son of King <a href="/wiki/James_VI_of_Scotland" class="mw-redirect" title="James VI of Scotland">James VI of Scotland</a> and <a href="/wiki/Anne_of_Denmark" title="Anne of Denmark">Anne of Denmark</a>, Charles was born in <a href="/wiki/Dunfermline_Palace" title="Dunfermline Palace">Dunfermline Palace</a>, <a href="/wiki/Fife" title="Fife">Fife</a>, on 19 November 1600.<sup id="cite_ref-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-2">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup> At a <a href="/wiki/Protestant" class="mw-redirect" title="Protestant">Protestant</a> ceremony in the <a href="/wiki/Chapel_Royal" title="Chapel Royal">Chapel Royal</a> of <a href="/wiki/Holyrood_Palace" title="Holyrood Palace">Holyrood Palace</a> in <a href="/wiki/Edinburgh" title="Edinburgh">Edinburgh</a> on 23 December 1600, he was baptised by <a href="/wiki/David_Lindsay_(d._1613)" class="mw-redirect" title="David Lindsay (d. 1613)">David Lindsay</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bishop_of_Ross_(Scotland)" title="Bishop of Ross (Scotland)">Bishop of Ross</a>, and created <a href="/wiki/Duke_of_Albany" title="Duke of Albany">Duke of Albany</a>, the traditional title of the second son of the King of Scotland, with the <a href="/wiki/Subsidiary_title" title="Subsidiary title">subsidiary titles</a> of <a href="/wiki/Earl_of_Ormond_(Scottish)" class="mw-redirect" title="Earl of Ormond (Scottish)">Marquess of Ormond</a>, <a href="/wiki/Earl_of_Ross" title="Earl of Ross">Earl of Ross</a> and Lord Ardmannoch.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGregg19814–5_3-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGregg19814–5-3">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>James VI was the first cousin twice removed of Queen <a href="/wiki/Elizabeth_I_of_England" title="Elizabeth I of England">Elizabeth I of England</a>, and when she died childless in March 1603, he became <a href="/wiki/King_of_England" class="mw-redirect" title="King of England">King of England</a> as James&#160;I. Charles was a weak and sickly infant, and while his parents and older siblings left for <a href="/wiki/England" title="England">England</a> in April and early June that year, due to his fragile health,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECust20052_4-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECust20052-4">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup> he remained in Scotland with his father's friend <a href="/wiki/Alexander_Seton,_1st_Earl_of_Dunfermline" title="Alexander Seton, 1st Earl of Dunfermline">Lord Fyvie</a>, appointed as his guardian.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECarlton19952_5-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECarlton19952-5">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>By 1604, when Charles was three-and-a-half, he was able to walk the length of the great hall at Dunfermline Palace without assistance, and it was decided that he was strong enough to make the journey to England to be reunited with his family. In mid-July 1604, Charles left Dunfermline for England where he was to spend most of the rest of his life.<sup id="cite_ref-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-6">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup> In England, Charles was placed under the charge of Elizabeth, Lady Carey, the wife of courtier <a href="/wiki/Robert_Carey,_1st_Earl_of_Monmouth" title="Robert Carey, 1st Earl of Monmouth">Sir Robert Carey</a>, who put him in boots made of Spanish leather and brass to help strengthen his weak ankles.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGregg198111_7-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGregg198111-7">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup> His speech development was also slow, and he retained a stammer, or hesitant speech, for the rest of his life.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGregg198112_8-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGregg198112-8">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Charles_I_(young).jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7d/Charles_I_%28young%29.jpg/170px-Charles_I_%28young%29.jpg" decoding="async" width="170" height="254" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7d/Charles_I_%28young%29.jpg/255px-Charles_I_%28young%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7d/Charles_I_%28young%29.jpg/340px-Charles_I_%28young%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1000" data-file-height="1497" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Charles_I_(young).jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Portrait by <a href="/wiki/Robert_Peake_the_elder" class="mw-redirect" title="Robert Peake the elder">Robert Peake</a>, c. 1610</div></div></div>
<p>In January 1605, Charles was created <a href="/wiki/Duke_of_York" title="Duke of York">Duke of York</a>, as is customary in the case of the English sovereign's second son, and made a <a href="/wiki/Knight_of_the_Bath" class="mw-redirect" title="Knight of the Bath">Knight of the Bath</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGregg198113_9-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGregg198113-9">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Murray_(provost_of_Eton)" title="Thomas Murray (provost of Eton)">Thomas Murray</a>, a <a href="/wiki/Presbyterianism" title="Presbyterianism">presbyterian</a> Scot, was appointed as a tutor.<sup id="cite_ref-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-10">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup> Charles learnt the usual subjects of classics, languages, mathematics and religion.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECarlton199516_11-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECarlton199516-11">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup> In 1611, he was made a <a href="/wiki/Knight_of_the_Garter" class="mw-redirect" title="Knight of the Garter">Knight of the Garter</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGregg198122_12-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGregg198122-12">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Eventually, Charles apparently conquered his physical infirmity,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGregg198122_12-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGregg198122-12">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup> which might have been caused by <a href="/wiki/Rickets" title="Rickets">rickets</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGregg198111_7-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGregg198111-7">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup> He became an adept horseman and marksman, and took up fencing.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECarlton199516_11-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECarlton199516-11">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup> Even so, his public profile remained low in contrast to that of his physically stronger and taller<sup id="cite_ref-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-13">&#91;b&#93;</a></sup> elder brother, <a href="/wiki/Henry_Frederick,_Prince_of_Wales" title="Henry Frederick, Prince of Wales">Henry Frederick, Prince of Wales</a>, whom Charles adored and attempted to emulate.<sup id="cite_ref-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-14">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup> However, in early November 1612, Henry died at the age of 18 of what is suspected to have been <a href="/wiki/Typhoid" class="mw-redirect" title="Typhoid">typhoid</a> (or possibly <a href="/wiki/Porphyria" title="Porphyria">porphyria</a>).<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGregg198129_15-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGregg198129-15">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup> Charles, who turned 12 two weeks later, became <a href="/wiki/Heir_apparent" title="Heir apparent">heir apparent</a>. As the eldest surviving son of the sovereign, Charles automatically gained several titles (including <a href="/wiki/Duke_of_Cornwall" title="Duke of Cornwall">Duke of Cornwall</a> and <a href="/wiki/Duke_of_Rothesay" title="Duke of Rothesay">Duke of Rothesay</a>). Four years later, in November 1616, he was created <a href="/wiki/Prince_of_Wales" title="Prince of Wales">Prince of Wales</a> and <a href="/wiki/Earl_of_Chester" title="Earl of Chester">Earl of Chester</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGregg198147_16-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGregg198147-16">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Heir_apparent">Heir apparent</span></h2>
<p>In 1613, Charles's sister <a href="/wiki/Elizabeth_of_Bohemia" class="mw-redirect" title="Elizabeth of Bohemia">Elizabeth</a> married <a href="/wiki/Frederick_V,_Elector_Palatine" class="mw-redirect" title="Frederick V, Elector Palatine">Frederick V, Elector Palatine</a>, and moved to <a href="/wiki/Heidelberg" title="Heidelberg">Heidelberg</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHibbert196824_17-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHibbert196824-17">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup> In 1617, the <a href="/wiki/House_of_Habsburg" title="House of Habsburg">Habsburg</a> <a href="/wiki/Ferdinand_II,_Holy_Roman_Emperor" title="Ferdinand II, Holy Roman Emperor">Archduke Ferdinand of Austria</a>, a Catholic, was elected <a href="/wiki/King_of_Bohemia" class="mw-redirect" title="King of Bohemia">king of Bohemia</a>. The following year, the <a href="/wiki/Bohemian_Revolt" title="Bohemian Revolt">Bohemians rebelled</a>, <a href="/wiki/Defenestrations_of_Prague#Second_Defenestration_of_Prague" title="Defenestrations of Prague">defenestrating the Catholic governors</a>. In August 1619, the Bohemian <a href="/wiki/Diet_(assembly)" title="Diet (assembly)">diet</a> chose as their monarch Frederick&#160;V, who was leader of the <a href="/wiki/Protestant_Union" title="Protestant Union">Protestant Union</a>, while Ferdinand was elected <a href="/wiki/Holy_Roman_Emperor" title="Holy Roman Emperor">Holy Roman Emperor</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Imperial_election_of_1619" class="mw-redirect" title="Imperial election of 1619">imperial election</a>. Frederick's acceptance of the Bohemian crown in defiance of the emperor marked the beginning of the turmoil that would develop into the <a href="/wiki/Thirty_Years%27_War" title="Thirty Years&#39; War">Thirty Years' War</a>. The conflict, originally confined to Bohemia, spiralled into a wider European war, which the <a href="/wiki/Parliament_of_England" title="Parliament of England">English Parliament</a> and public quickly grew to see as a polarised continental struggle between Catholics and Protestants.<sup id="cite_ref-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-18">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup> In 1620, Charles's brother-in-law, Frederick V, was defeated at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_White_Mountain" title="Battle of White Mountain">Battle of White Mountain</a> near <a href="/wiki/Prague" title="Prague">Prague</a> and his hereditary lands in the <a href="/wiki/Electoral_Palatinate" title="Electoral Palatinate">Electoral Palatinate</a> were <a href="/wiki/Palatinate_campaign" title="Palatinate campaign">invaded by a Habsburg force</a> from the <a href="/wiki/Spanish_Netherlands" title="Spanish Netherlands">Spanish Netherlands</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-19">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup> James, however, had been seeking marriage between the new Prince of Wales and Ferdinand's niece, Habsburg princess <a href="/wiki/Maria_Anna_of_Spain" title="Maria Anna of Spain">Maria Anna of Spain</a>, and began to see the <a href="/wiki/Spanish_match" title="Spanish match">Spanish match</a> as a possible diplomatic means of achieving peace in Europe.<sup id="cite_ref-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Unfortunately for James, negotiation with Spain proved generally unpopular, both with the public and with James's court.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECoward2003152_21-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECoward2003152-21">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup> The English Parliament was actively hostile towards Spain and Catholicism, and thus, when called by James in 1621, the members hoped for an enforcement of <a href="/wiki/Recusancy" title="Recusancy">recusancy</a> laws, a naval campaign against Spain, and a Protestant marriage for the Prince of Wales.<sup id="cite_ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup> James's <a href="/wiki/Lord_Chancellor" title="Lord Chancellor">Lord Chancellor</a>, <a href="/wiki/Francis_Bacon" title="Francis Bacon">Francis Bacon</a>, was impeached before the <a href="/wiki/House_of_Lords" title="House of Lords">House of Lords</a> for corruption.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECarlton199531_23-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECarlton199531-23">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Impeachment" title="Impeachment">impeachment</a> was the first since 1459 without the king's official sanction in the form of a <a href="/wiki/Bill_of_attainder" title="Bill of attainder">bill of attainder</a>. The incident set an important precedent as the process of impeachment would later be used against Charles and his supporters:  <a href="/wiki/George_Villiers,_1st_Duke_of_Buckingham" title="George Villiers, 1st Duke of Buckingham">the Duke of Buckingham</a>, <a href="/wiki/William_Laud" title="William Laud">Archbishop William Laud</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Wentworth,_1st_Earl_of_Strafford" title="Thomas Wentworth, 1st Earl of Strafford">the Earl of Strafford</a>. James insisted that the <a href="/wiki/House_of_Commons_of_England" title="House of Commons of England">House of Commons</a> be concerned exclusively with domestic affairs, while the members protested that they had the privilege of free speech within the Commons' walls, demanding war with Spain and a Protestant Princess of Wales.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECust20058_24-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECust20058-24">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup> Charles, like his father, considered the discussion of his marriage in the Commons impertinent and an infringement of his father's <a href="/wiki/Royal_prerogative" title="Royal prerogative">royal prerogative</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECust20055–9_25-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECust20055–9-25">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup> In January 1622, James dissolved Parliament, angry at what he perceived as the members' impudence and intransigence.<sup id="cite_ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Charles_I_(Prince_of_Wales).jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/11/Charles_I_%28Prince_of_Wales%29.jpg/170px-Charles_I_%28Prince_of_Wales%29.jpg" decoding="async" width="170" height="228" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/11/Charles_I_%28Prince_of_Wales%29.jpg/255px-Charles_I_%28Prince_of_Wales%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/11/Charles_I_%28Prince_of_Wales%29.jpg/340px-Charles_I_%28Prince_of_Wales%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="670" data-file-height="900" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Charles_I_(Prince_of_Wales).jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Portrait of Charles as Prince of Wales after <a href="/wiki/Daniel_Mytens" class="mw-redirect" title="Daniel Mytens">Daniel Mytens</a>, c. 1623</div></div></div>
<p>Charles and the Duke of Buckingham, James's favourite and a man who had great influence over the prince,<sup id="cite_ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup> travelled incognito to Spain in February 1623 to try to reach agreement on the long-pending Spanish match.<sup id="cite_ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup> In the end, however, the trip was an embarrassing failure.<sup id="cite_ref-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-29">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup> The Infanta thought Charles was little more than an infidel, and the Spanish at first demanded that he convert to Roman Catholicism as a condition of the match.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGregg198184_30-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGregg198184-30">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup> The Spanish insisted on toleration of Catholics in England and the repeal of the <a href="/wiki/Penal_law_(Britain)" class="mw-redirect" title="Penal law (Britain)">penal laws</a>, which Charles knew would never be agreed by Parliament, and that the Infanta remain in Spain for a year after any wedding to ensure that England complied with all the terms of the treaty.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGregg198185–87_31-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGregg198185–87-31">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup> A personal quarrel erupted between Buckingham and the <a href="/wiki/Gaspar_de_Guzm%C3%A1n,_Count-Duke_of_Olivares" title="Gaspar de Guzmán, Count-Duke of Olivares">Count of Olivares</a>, the Spanish chief minister, and so Charles conducted the ultimately futile negotiations personally.<sup id="cite_ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup> When Charles returned to London in October, without a bride and to a rapturous and relieved public welcome,<sup id="cite_ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup> he and Buckingham pushed a reluctant King James to declare war on Spain.<sup id="cite_ref-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-34">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>With the encouragement of his Protestant advisers, James summoned the English Parliament in 1624 so that he could request subsidies for a war. Charles and Buckingham supported the impeachment of the <a href="/wiki/Lord_Treasurer" class="mw-redirect" title="Lord Treasurer">Lord Treasurer</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lionel_Cranfield,_1st_Earl_of_Middlesex" title="Lionel Cranfield, 1st Earl of Middlesex">Lionel Cranfield, 1st Earl of Middlesex</a>, who opposed war on grounds of cost and who quickly fell in much the same manner as Bacon had.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGregg198197–99_35-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGregg198197–99-35">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup> James told Buckingham he was a fool, and presciently warned his son that he would live to regret the revival of impeachment as a parliamentary tool.<sup id="cite_ref-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-36">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup> An under-funded makeshift army under <a href="/wiki/Ernst_von_Mansfeld" title="Ernst von Mansfeld">Ernst von Mansfeld</a> set off to recover the Palatinate, but it was so poorly provisioned that it never advanced beyond the Dutch coast.<sup id="cite_ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>By 1624, James was growing ill, and as a result was finding it difficult to control Parliament. By the time of his death in March 1625, Charles and the Duke of Buckingham had already assumed <i>de facto</i> control of the kingdom.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETrevelyan1922130_38-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETrevelyan1922130-38">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_reign">Early reign</span></h2>
<p>With the failure of the Spanish match, Charles and Buckingham turned their attention to France.<sup id="cite_ref-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-39">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup> On 1 May 1625 Charles was <a href="/wiki/Married_by_proxy" class="mw-redirect" title="Married by proxy">married by proxy</a> to the fifteen-year-old French princess <a href="/wiki/Henrietta_Maria" class="mw-redirect" title="Henrietta Maria">Henrietta Maria</a> in front of the doors of the <a href="/wiki/Notre_Dame_de_Paris" class="mw-redirect" title="Notre Dame de Paris">Notre Dame de Paris</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup> Charles had seen Henrietta Maria in Paris while en route to Spain.<sup id="cite_ref-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-41">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup> The married couple met in person on 13 June 1625 in <a href="/wiki/Canterbury" title="Canterbury">Canterbury</a>. Charles delayed the opening of his first Parliament until after the marriage was consummated, to forestall any opposition.<sup id="cite_ref-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-42">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup> Many members of the Commons were opposed to the king's marriage to a Roman Catholic, fearing that Charles would lift restrictions on Catholic recusants and undermine the official establishment of the reformed <a href="/wiki/Church_of_England" title="Church of England">Church of England</a>. Although he told Parliament that he would not relax religious restrictions, he promised to do exactly that in a secret marriage treaty with his brother-in-law <a href="/wiki/Louis_XIII_of_France" title="Louis XIII of France">Louis XIII of France</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECarlton199555,_70_43-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECarlton199555,_70-43">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup> Moreover, the treaty loaned to the French seven English naval ships that would be used to suppress the Protestant Huguenots at <a href="/wiki/La_Rochelle" title="La Rochelle">La Rochelle</a> in September 1625.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEQuintrell199316,_21_44-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEQuintrell199316,_21-44">&#91;42&#93;</a></sup> Charles was <a href="/wiki/Coronation_of_the_British_monarch" title="Coronation of the British monarch">crowned</a> on 2 February 1626 at <a href="/wiki/Westminster_Abbey" title="Westminster Abbey">Westminster Abbey</a>, but without his wife at his side because she refused to participate in a Protestant religious ceremony.<sup id="cite_ref-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-45">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Distrust of Charles's religious policies increased with his support of a controversial <a href="/wiki/History_of_Calvinist-Arminian_debate" class="mw-redirect" title="History of Calvinist-Arminian debate">anti-Calvinist</a> ecclesiastic, <a href="/wiki/Richard_Montagu" title="Richard Montagu">Richard Montagu</a>, who was in disrepute among the <a href="/wiki/Puritan" class="mw-redirect" title="Puritan">Puritans</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGregg1981130–131_46-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGregg1981130–131-46">&#91;44&#93;</a></sup> In his pamphlet <i>A New Gag for an Old Goose</i> (1624), a reply to the Catholic pamphlet <i>A New Gag for the New Gospel</i>, Montagu argued against <a href="/wiki/Calvinist" class="mw-redirect" title="Calvinist">Calvinist</a> <a href="/wiki/Predestination" title="Predestination">predestination</a>, the doctrine that <a href="/wiki/Salvation" title="Salvation">salvation</a> and <a href="/wiki/Damnation" title="Damnation">damnation</a> were preordained by God. Anti-Calvinists—known as <a href="/wiki/Arminians" class="mw-redirect" title="Arminians">Arminians</a>—believed that human beings could influence their own fate through the exercise of free will.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECust200584–86_47-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECust200584–86-47">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup> Arminian divines had been one of the few sources of support for Charles's proposed Spanish marriage.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECoward2003153_48-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECoward2003153-48">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup> With the support of King James, Montagu produced another pamphlet, entitled <i><a href="/wiki/Richard_Montagu#The_Appello" title="Richard Montagu">Appello Caesarem</a></i>, in 1625 shortly after the old king's death and Charles's accession. To protect Montagu from the stricture of Puritan members of Parliament, Charles made the cleric one of his royal chaplains, increasing many Puritans' suspicions that Charles favoured Arminianism as a clandestine attempt to aid the resurgence of Catholicism.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGregg1981131_49-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGregg1981131-49">&#91;47&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Rather than direct involvement in the European land war, the English Parliament preferred a relatively inexpensive naval attack on Spanish colonies in the <a href="/wiki/New_World" title="New World">New World</a>, hoping for the capture of the <a href="/wiki/Spanish_treasure_fleet" title="Spanish treasure fleet">Spanish treasure fleets</a>. Parliament voted to grant a subsidy of £140,000, which was an insufficient sum for Charles's war plans.<sup id="cite_ref-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-50">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup> Moreover, the House of Commons limited its authorisation for royal collection of <a href="/wiki/Tonnage_and_poundage" title="Tonnage and poundage">tonnage and poundage</a> (two varieties of customs duties) to a period of one year, although previous sovereigns since <a href="/wiki/Henry_VI_of_England" title="Henry VI of England">Henry VI</a> had been granted the right for life.<sup id="cite_ref-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-51">&#91;49&#93;</a></sup> In this manner, Parliament could delay approval of the rates until after a full-scale review of customs revenue.<sup id="cite_ref-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-52">&#91;50&#93;</a></sup> The bill made no progress in the House of Lords past its <a href="/wiki/First_reading" class="mw-redirect" title="First reading">first reading</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESmith199954,_114_53-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESmith199954,_114-53">&#91;51&#93;</a></sup> Although no Parliamentary Act for the levy of tonnage and poundage was obtained, Charles continued to collect the duties.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGregg1981138_54-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGregg1981138-54">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:King_Charles_I_by_Gerrit_van_Honthorst_sm.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/52/King_Charles_I_by_Gerrit_van_Honthorst_sm.jpg/220px-King_Charles_I_by_Gerrit_van_Honthorst_sm.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="265" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/52/King_Charles_I_by_Gerrit_van_Honthorst_sm.jpg/330px-King_Charles_I_by_Gerrit_van_Honthorst_sm.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/52/King_Charles_I_by_Gerrit_van_Honthorst_sm.jpg/440px-King_Charles_I_by_Gerrit_van_Honthorst_sm.jpg 2x" data-file-width="599" data-file-height="721" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:King_Charles_I_by_Gerrit_van_Honthorst_sm.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Portrait by <a href="/wiki/Gerrit_van_Honthorst" class="mw-redirect" title="Gerrit van Honthorst">Gerrit van Honthorst</a>, 1628</div></div></div>
<p>A poorly conceived and executed <a href="/wiki/Cadiz_Expedition_(1625)" class="mw-redirect" title="Cadiz Expedition (1625)">naval expedition against Spain</a> under the leadership of Buckingham went badly, and the House of Commons began proceedings for the impeachment of the duke.<sup id="cite_ref-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-55">&#91;53&#93;</a></sup> In May 1626, Charles nominated Buckingham as <a href="/wiki/Chancellor_of_Cambridge_University" class="mw-redirect" title="Chancellor of Cambridge University">Chancellor of Cambridge University</a> in a show of support,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGregg1981150_56-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGregg1981150-56">&#91;54&#93;</a></sup> and had two members who had spoken against Buckingham—<a href="/wiki/Dudley_Digges" title="Dudley Digges">Dudley Digges</a> and <a href="/wiki/John_Eliot_(statesman)" title="John Eliot (statesman)">Sir John Eliot</a>—arrested at the door of the House. The Commons was outraged by the imprisonment of two of their members, and after about a week in custody, both were released.<sup id="cite_ref-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-57">&#91;55&#93;</a></sup> On 12 June 1626, the Commons launched a direct protestation attacking Buckingham, stating,
"We protest before your Majesty and the whole world that until this great person be removed from intermeddling with the great affairs of state, we are out of hope of any good success; and do fear that any money we shall or can give will, through his misemployment, be turned rather to the hurt and prejudice of this your kingdom than otherwise, as by lamentable experience we have found those large supplies formerly and lately given."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELoades1974369–370_58-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELoades1974369–370-58">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup> Despite Parliament's protests, however, Charles refused to dismiss his friend, dismissing Parliament instead.<sup id="cite_ref-59" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-59">&#91;57&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Meanwhile, domestic quarrels between Charles and Henrietta Maria were souring the early years of their marriage. Disputes over her <a href="/wiki/Jointure" title="Jointure">jointure</a>, appointments to her household, and the practice of her religion culminated in the king expelling the vast majority of her French attendants in August 1626.<sup id="cite_ref-60" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-60">&#91;58&#93;</a></sup> Despite Charles's agreement to provide the French with English ships as a condition of marrying Henrietta Maria, in 1627 he launched <a href="/wiki/Siege_of_Saint-Martin-de-R%C3%A9_(1627)" class="mw-redirect" title="Siege of Saint-Martin-de-Ré (1627)">an attack on the French coast</a> to defend the Huguenots at La Rochelle.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHowat197435_61-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHowat197435-61">&#91;59&#93;</a></sup> The action, led by Buckingham, was ultimately unsuccessful. Buckingham's failure to protect the Huguenots—and his retreat from <a href="/wiki/Saint-Martin-de-R%C3%A9" title="Saint-Martin-de-Ré">Saint-Martin-de-Ré</a>—spurred Louis XIII's <a href="/wiki/Siege_of_La_Rochelle" title="Siege of La Rochelle">siege of La Rochelle</a> and furthered the English Parliament's and people's detestation of the duke.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGregg1981173–174_62-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGregg1981173–174-62">&#91;60&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Charles provoked further unrest by trying to raise money for the war through a "forced loan": a tax levied without parliamentary consent. In November 1627, the test case in the <a href="/wiki/King%27s_Bench" class="mw-redirect" title="King&#39;s Bench">King's Bench</a>, the "<a href="/wiki/Five_Knights%27_Case" class="mw-redirect" title="Five Knights&#39; Case">Five Knights' Case</a>", found that the king had a prerogative right to imprison without trial those who refused to pay the forced loan.<sup id="cite_ref-63" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-63">&#91;61&#93;</a></sup> Summoned again in March 1628, on 26 May Parliament adopted a <a href="/wiki/Petition_of_Right" title="Petition of Right">Petition of Right</a>, calling upon the king to acknowledge that he could not levy taxes without Parliament's consent, not impose martial law on civilians, not imprison them without due process, and not quarter troops in their homes.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGregg1981170–173_64-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGregg1981170–173-64">&#91;62&#93;</a></sup> Charles assented to the petition on 7 June,<sup id="cite_ref-65" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-65">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup> but by the end of the month he had prorogued Parliament and re-asserted his right to collect customs duties without authorisation from Parliament.<sup id="cite_ref-66" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-66">&#91;64&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>On 23 August 1628, Buckingham was assassinated.<sup id="cite_ref-67" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-67">&#91;65&#93;</a></sup> Charles was deeply distressed. According to <a href="/wiki/Edward_Hyde,_1st_Earl_of_Clarendon" title="Edward Hyde, 1st Earl of Clarendon">Edward Hyde, 1st Earl of Clarendon</a>, he "threw himself upon his bed, lamenting with much passion and with abundance of tears".<sup id="cite_ref-68" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-68">&#91;66&#93;</a></sup> He remained grieving in his room for two days.<sup id="cite_ref-69" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-69">&#91;67&#93;</a></sup> In contrast, the public rejoiced at Buckingham's death, which accentuated the gulf between the court and the nation, and between the Crown and the Commons.<sup id="cite_ref-70" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-70">&#91;68&#93;</a></sup> Although the death of Buckingham effectively ended the war with Spain and eliminated his leadership as an issue, it did not end the conflicts between Charles and Parliament.<sup id="cite_ref-71" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-71">&#91;69&#93;</a></sup> It did, however, coincide with an improvement in Charles's relationship with his wife, and by November 1628 their old quarrels were at an end.<sup id="cite_ref-72" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-72">&#91;70&#93;</a></sup> Perhaps Charles's emotional ties were transferred from Buckingham to Henrietta Maria.<sup id="cite_ref-73" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-73">&#91;71&#93;</a></sup> She became pregnant for the first time, and the bond between them grew stronger.<sup id="cite_ref-74" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-74">&#91;72&#93;</a></sup> Together, they embodied an image of virtue and family life, and their court became a model of formality and morality.<sup id="cite_ref-75" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-75">&#91;73&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Personal_rule">Personal rule</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Parliament_prorogued">Parliament prorogued</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Peter_Paul_Rubens_-_Landscape_with_Saint_George_and_the_Dragon_-_WGA20401.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/30/Peter_Paul_Rubens_-_Landscape_with_Saint_George_and_the_Dragon_-_WGA20401.jpg/220px-Peter_Paul_Rubens_-_Landscape_with_Saint_George_and_the_Dragon_-_WGA20401.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="146" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/30/Peter_Paul_Rubens_-_Landscape_with_Saint_George_and_the_Dragon_-_WGA20401.jpg/330px-Peter_Paul_Rubens_-_Landscape_with_Saint_George_and_the_Dragon_-_WGA20401.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/30/Peter_Paul_Rubens_-_Landscape_with_Saint_George_and_the_Dragon_-_WGA20401.jpg/440px-Peter_Paul_Rubens_-_Landscape_with_Saint_George_and_the_Dragon_-_WGA20401.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1148" data-file-height="760" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Peter_Paul_Rubens_-_Landscape_with_Saint_George_and_the_Dragon_-_WGA20401.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Charles depicted as a victorious and chivalrous <a href="/wiki/Saint_George" title="Saint George">Saint George</a> in an English landscape by <a href="/wiki/Peter_Paul_Rubens" title="Peter Paul Rubens">Rubens</a>, 1629–30.<sup id="cite_ref-79" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-79">&#91;c&#93;</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>In January 1629, Charles opened the second session of the English Parliament, which had been <a href="/wiki/Prorogued" class="mw-redirect" title="Prorogued">prorogued</a> in June 1628, with a moderate speech on the tonnage and poundage issue.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECust2005114–115_80-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECust2005114–115-80">&#91;77&#93;</a></sup> Members of the House of Commons began to voice opposition to Charles's policies in light of the case of <a href="/wiki/John_Rolle_(Parliamentarian)" title="John Rolle (Parliamentarian)">John Rolle</a>, a Member of Parliament whose goods had been confiscated for failing to pay tonnage and poundage.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEQuintrell199342_81-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEQuintrell199342-81">&#91;78&#93;</a></sup> Many MPs viewed the imposition of the tax as a breach of the Petition of Right. When Charles ordered a parliamentary adjournment on 2 March,<sup id="cite_ref-82" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-82">&#91;79&#93;</a></sup> members held the Speaker, <a href="/wiki/John_Finch,_1st_Baron_Finch" title="John Finch, 1st Baron Finch">Sir John Finch</a>, down in his chair so that the ending of the session could be delayed long enough for resolutions against Catholicism, Arminianism and tonnage and poundage to be read out and acclaimed by the chamber.<sup id="cite_ref-83" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-83">&#91;80&#93;</a></sup> The provocation was too much for Charles, who dissolved Parliament and had nine parliamentary leaders, including Sir John Eliot, imprisoned over the matter,<sup id="cite_ref-84" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-84">&#91;81&#93;</a></sup> thereby turning the men into martyrs,<sup id="cite_ref-85" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-85">&#91;82&#93;</a></sup> and giving popular cause to their protest.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECust2005121–122_86-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECust2005121–122-86">&#91;83&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Shortly after the prorogation, without the means in the foreseeable future to raise funds from Parliament for a European war, or the influence of Buckingham, Charles made <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_Suza" title="Treaty of Suza">peace with France</a> and Spain.<sup id="cite_ref-87" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-87">&#91;84&#93;</a></sup> The following eleven years, during which Charles ruled England without a Parliament, are referred to as the <a href="/wiki/Personal_rule_of_Charles_I_(1629%E2%80%931640)" class="mw-redirect" title="Personal rule of Charles I (1629–1640)">personal rule</a> or the "eleven years' tyranny".<sup id="cite_ref-88" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-88">&#91;85&#93;</a></sup> Ruling without Parliament was not exceptional, and was supported by precedent.<sup id="cite_ref-90" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-90">&#91;d&#93;</a></sup> Only Parliament, however, could legally raise taxes, and without it Charles's capacity to acquire funds for his treasury was limited to his customary rights and prerogatives.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEStarkey2006104_91-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEStarkey2006104-91">&#91;87&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Finances">Finances</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Charles_I_AR_Sixpence_722625.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f2/Charles_I_AR_Sixpence_722625.jpg/220px-Charles_I_AR_Sixpence_722625.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="104" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f2/Charles_I_AR_Sixpence_722625.jpg/330px-Charles_I_AR_Sixpence_722625.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f2/Charles_I_AR_Sixpence_722625.jpg/440px-Charles_I_AR_Sixpence_722625.jpg 2x" data-file-width="800" data-file-height="377" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Charles_I_AR_Sixpence_722625.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Sixpence_(British_coin)" title="Sixpence (British coin)">Sixpence</a> of Charles I, inscribed: <i>CAROLUS <a href="/wiki/By_the_Grace_of_God" title="By the Grace of God">D(EI) G(RATIA)</a> MAG(NAE) BRIT(ANNIAE) FR(ANCIAE) ET HIB(ERNIAE) REX</i> ("Charles, by the grace of God, King of Great Britain, of France and of Ireland")</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Post_medieval_coin,_Rose_farthing_of_Charles_I_(FindID_619759).jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/26/Post_medieval_coin%2C_Rose_farthing_of_Charles_I_%28FindID_619759%29.jpg/220px-Post_medieval_coin%2C_Rose_farthing_of_Charles_I_%28FindID_619759%29.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="118" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/26/Post_medieval_coin%2C_Rose_farthing_of_Charles_I_%28FindID_619759%29.jpg/330px-Post_medieval_coin%2C_Rose_farthing_of_Charles_I_%28FindID_619759%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/26/Post_medieval_coin%2C_Rose_farthing_of_Charles_I_%28FindID_619759%29.jpg/440px-Post_medieval_coin%2C_Rose_farthing_of_Charles_I_%28FindID_619759%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1150" data-file-height="618" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Post_medieval_coin,_Rose_farthing_of_Charles_I_(FindID_619759).jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Farthing_(English_coin)#Rose_farthing" title="Farthing (English coin)">Farthing</a> of Charles I, showing a crown over two <a href="/wiki/Sceptre" title="Sceptre">sceptres</a> in saltire on the obverse. The two sceptres represent the two kingdoms of England and Scotland.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWeightman1906185_92-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWeightman1906185-92">&#91;88&#93;</a></sup></div></div></div> 
<p>A large fiscal deficit had arisen in the reigns of Elizabeth I and James I.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGregg198140_93-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGregg198140-93">&#91;89&#93;</a></sup> Notwithstanding Buckingham's short-lived campaigns against both Spain and France, there was little financial capacity for Charles to wage wars overseas. Throughout his reign Charles was obliged to rely primarily on volunteer forces for defence and on diplomatic efforts to support his sister, Elizabeth, and his foreign policy objective for the restoration of the Palatinate.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESharpe1992509–536,_541–545,_825–834_94-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESharpe1992509–536,_541–545,_825–834-94">&#91;90&#93;</a></sup> England was still the least taxed country in Europe, with no official excise and no regular direct taxation.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGregg1981220_95-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGregg1981220-95">&#91;91&#93;</a></sup> To raise revenue without reconvening Parliament, Charles resurrected an all-but-forgotten law called the "Distraint of Knighthood", in abeyance for over a century, which required any man who earned £40 or more from land each year to present himself at the king's coronation to be knighted. Relying on this old statute, Charles fined individuals who had failed to attend his coronation in 1626.<sup id="cite_ref-96" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-96">&#91;92&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-98" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-98">&#91;e&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The chief tax imposed by Charles was a feudal levy known as <a href="/wiki/Ship_money" title="Ship money">ship money</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-99" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-99">&#91;94&#93;</a></sup> which proved even more unpopular, and lucrative, than tonnage and poundage before it. Previously, collection of ship money had been authorised only during wars, and only on coastal regions. Charles, however, argued that there was no legal bar to collecting the tax for defence during peacetime and throughout the whole of the kingdom. Ship money, paid directly to the Treasury of the Navy, provided between £150,000 to £200,000 annually between 1634 and 1638, after which yields declined.<sup id="cite_ref-100" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-100">&#91;95&#93;</a></sup> Opposition to ship money steadily grew, but the 12 common law judges of England declared that the tax was within the king's prerogative, though some of them had reservations.<sup id="cite_ref-101" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-101">&#91;96&#93;</a></sup> The prosecution of <a href="/wiki/John_Hampden" title="John Hampden">John Hampden</a> for non-payment in 1637–38 provided a platform for popular protest, and the judges found against Hampden only by the narrow margin of 7–5.<sup id="cite_ref-102" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-102">&#91;97&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The king also derived money through the granting of monopolies, despite a <a href="/wiki/Statute_of_Monopolies" title="Statute of Monopolies">statute forbidding such action</a>, which, though inefficient, raised an estimated £100,000 a year in the late 1630s.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELoades1974385_103-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELoades1974385-103">&#91;98&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-105" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-105">&#91;f&#93;</a></sup>  One such monopoly was for soap, pejoratively referred to as "<a href="/wiki/Popish_soap" title="Popish soap">popish soap</a>" because some of its backers were Catholics.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEYoung199797_106-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEYoung199797-106">&#91;100&#93;</a></sup> Charles also raised funds from the Scottish nobility, at the price of considerable acrimony, by the Act of Revocation (1625), whereby all gifts of royal or church land made to the nobility since 1540 were revoked, with continued ownership being subject to an annual rent.<sup id="cite_ref-107" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-107">&#91;101&#93;</a></sup> In addition, the boundaries of the <a href="/wiki/Royal_forest" title="Royal forest">royal forests</a> in England were restored to their ancient limits as part of a scheme to maximise income by exploiting the land and fining land users within the reasserted boundaries for encroachment.<sup id="cite_ref-108" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-108">&#91;102&#93;</a></sup> The focus of the programme was disafforestation and sale of forest lands for conversion to pasture and arable farming, or in the case of the <a href="/wiki/Forest_of_Dean" title="Forest of Dean">Forest of Dean</a>, development for the iron industry. Disafforestation frequently caused riots and disturbances including those known as the <a href="/wiki/Western_Rising_and_disafforestation_riots" title="Western Rising and disafforestation riots">Western Rising</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESharp198082_ff_109-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESharp198082_ff-109">&#91;103&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Against the background of this unrest, Charles faced bankruptcy in mid-1640. The City of London, preoccupied with its own grievances, refused to make any loans to the king, as did foreign powers. In this extremity, in July Charles seized silver bullion worth £130,000 held in trust at the <a href="/wiki/Royal_Mint" title="Royal Mint">mint</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Tower_of_London" title="Tower of London">Tower of London</a>, promising its later return at 8% interest to its owners.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGregg1981312–313_110-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGregg1981312–313-110">&#91;104&#93;</a></sup> In August, after the <a href="/wiki/East_India_Company" title="East India Company">East India Company</a> refused to grant a loan,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESharpe1992906_111-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESharpe1992906-111">&#91;105&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Lord_Cottington" class="mw-redirect" title="Lord Cottington">Lord Cottington</a> seized the company's stock of pepper and spices and sold it for £60,000 (far below its market value), promising to refund the money with interest later.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGregg1981314_112-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGregg1981314-112">&#91;106&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Religious_conflicts">Religious conflicts</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Anthony_van_Dyck_-_Charles_I_(1600-49)_with_M._de_St_Antoine_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6c/Anthony_van_Dyck_-_Charles_I_%281600-49%29_with_M._de_St_Antoine_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg/220px-Anthony_van_Dyck_-_Charles_I_%281600-49%29_with_M._de_St_Antoine_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="303" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6c/Anthony_van_Dyck_-_Charles_I_%281600-49%29_with_M._de_St_Antoine_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg/330px-Anthony_van_Dyck_-_Charles_I_%281600-49%29_with_M._de_St_Antoine_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6c/Anthony_van_Dyck_-_Charles_I_%281600-49%29_with_M._de_St_Antoine_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg/440px-Anthony_van_Dyck_-_Charles_I_%281600-49%29_with_M._de_St_Antoine_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3806" data-file-height="5238" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Anthony_van_Dyck_-_Charles_I_(1600-49)_with_M._de_St_Antoine_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i><a href="/wiki/Charles_I_with_M._de_St_Antoine" title="Charles I with M. de St Antoine">Charles I with M. de St Antoine</a></i> by <a href="/wiki/Anthony_van_Dyck" title="Anthony van Dyck">Anthony van Dyck</a>, 1633</div></div></div>
<p>Throughout Charles's reign, the issue of how far the <a href="/wiki/English_Reformation" title="English Reformation">English Reformation</a> should progress was constantly in the forefront of political debate. <a href="/wiki/Arminianism" title="Arminianism">Arminian</a> theology emphasised clerical authority and the individual's ability to reject or accept salvation, which opponents viewed as heretical and a potential vehicle for the reintroduction of Roman Catholicism. <a href="/wiki/Puritan" class="mw-redirect" title="Puritan">Puritan</a> reformers thought Charles was too sympathetic to the teachings of Arminianism, which they considered irreligious, and opposed his desire to move the Church of England in a more traditional and sacramental direction.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECust200597–103_113-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECust200597–103-113">&#91;107&#93;</a></sup> In addition, his Protestant subjects followed the European war closely<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDonaghan199565–100_114-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDonaghan199565–100-114">&#91;108&#93;</a></sup> and grew increasingly dismayed by Charles's diplomacy with Spain and his failure to support the Protestant cause abroad effectively.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHowat197440–46_115-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHowat197440–46-115">&#91;109&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1633, Charles appointed <a href="/wiki/William_Laud" title="William Laud">William Laud</a> <a href="/wiki/Archbishop_of_Canterbury" title="Archbishop of Canterbury">Archbishop of Canterbury</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECust2005133_116-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECust2005133-116">&#91;110&#93;</a></sup> They initiated a series of reforms aimed at ensuring religious uniformity by restricting non-conformist preachers, insisting the <a href="/wiki/Liturgy" title="Liturgy">liturgy</a> be celebrated as prescribed by the <a href="/wiki/Book_of_Common_Prayer" title="Book of Common Prayer">Book of Common Prayer</a>, organising the internal architecture of English churches to emphasise the sacrament of the altar, and re-issuing King James's <a href="/wiki/Declaration_of_Sports" title="Declaration of Sports">Declaration of Sports</a>, which permitted secular activities on the sabbath.<sup id="cite_ref-117" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-117">&#91;111&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Feoffees_for_Impropriations" class="mw-redirect" title="Feoffees for Impropriations">Feoffees for Impropriations</a>, an organisation that bought <a href="/wiki/Benefice" title="Benefice">benefices</a> and <a href="/wiki/Advowson" title="Advowson">advowsons</a> so that Puritans could be appointed to them, was dissolved.<sup id="cite_ref-118" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-118">&#91;112&#93;</a></sup> Laud prosecuted those who opposed his reforms in the <a href="/wiki/Court_of_High_Commission" title="Court of High Commission">Court of High Commission</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Star_Chamber" title="Star Chamber">Star Chamber</a>, the two most powerful courts in the land.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECoward2003175–176_119-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECoward2003175–176-119">&#91;113&#93;</a></sup> The courts became feared for their censorship of opposing religious views and unpopular among the propertied classes for inflicting degrading punishments on gentlemen.<sup id="cite_ref-120" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-120">&#91;114&#93;</a></sup> For example, in 1637 <a href="/wiki/William_Prynne" title="William Prynne">William Prynne</a>, <a href="/wiki/Henry_Burton_(Puritan)" class="mw-redirect" title="Henry Burton (Puritan)">Henry Burton</a> and <a href="/wiki/John_Bastwick" title="John Bastwick">John Bastwick</a> were <a href="/wiki/Pillory" title="Pillory">pilloried</a>, whipped and mutilated by <a href="/wiki/Cropping_(punishment)" title="Cropping (punishment)">cropping</a> and imprisoned indefinitely for publishing anti-episcopal pamphlets.<sup id="cite_ref-121" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-121">&#91;115&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Sir_Anthony_Van_Dyck_-_Charles_I_(1600-49)_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b0/Sir_Anthony_Van_Dyck_-_Charles_I_%281600-49%29_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg/220px-Sir_Anthony_Van_Dyck_-_Charles_I_%281600-49%29_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="184" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b0/Sir_Anthony_Van_Dyck_-_Charles_I_%281600-49%29_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg/330px-Sir_Anthony_Van_Dyck_-_Charles_I_%281600-49%29_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b0/Sir_Anthony_Van_Dyck_-_Charles_I_%281600-49%29_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg/440px-Sir_Anthony_Van_Dyck_-_Charles_I_%281600-49%29_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg 2x" data-file-width="5076" data-file-height="4252" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Sir_Anthony_Van_Dyck_-_Charles_I_(1600-49)_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i><a href="/wiki/Charles_I_in_Three_Positions" title="Charles I in Three Positions">Charles I in Three Positions</a></i> by van Dyck, 1635–36</div></div></div>
<p>When Charles attempted to impose his religious policies in Scotland he faced numerous difficulties. Although born in Scotland, Charles had become estranged from his northern kingdom; his first visit since early childhood was for his Scottish coronation in 1633.<sup id="cite_ref-122" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-122">&#91;116&#93;</a></sup> To the dismay of the Scots, who had removed many traditional rituals from their liturgical practice, Charles insisted that the coronation be conducted using the <a href="/wiki/Anglican" class="mw-redirect" title="Anglican">Anglican</a> rite.<sup id="cite_ref-123" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-123">&#91;117&#93;</a></sup> In 1637, the king ordered the use of a new prayer book in Scotland that was almost identical to the English Book of Common Prayer, without consulting either the Scottish Parliament or the <a href="/wiki/Kirk" title="Kirk">Kirk</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-124" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-124">&#91;118&#93;</a></sup> Although it had been written, under Charles's direction, by Scottish bishops, many Scots resisted it, seeing the new prayer book as a vehicle for introducing Anglicanism to Scotland.<sup id="cite_ref-125" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-125">&#91;119&#93;</a></sup> On 23 July, <a href="/wiki/Jenny_Geddes" title="Jenny Geddes">riots</a> erupted in Edinburgh upon the first Sunday of the prayer book's usage, and unrest spread throughout the Kirk. The public began to mobilise around a reaffirmation of the <a href="/wiki/National_Covenant" class="mw-redirect" title="National Covenant">National Covenant</a>, whose signatories pledged to uphold the reformed religion of Scotland and reject any innovations that were not authorised by Kirk and Parliament.<sup id="cite_ref-126" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-126">&#91;120&#93;</a></sup> When the <a href="/wiki/General_Assembly_of_the_Church_of_Scotland" title="General Assembly of the Church of Scotland">General Assembly of the Church of Scotland</a> met in November 1638, it condemned the new prayer book, abolished <a href="/wiki/Episcopal_polity" title="Episcopal polity">episcopal church government</a> by bishops, and adopted <a href="/wiki/Presbyterian_polity" title="Presbyterian polity">presbyterian</a> government by elders and deacons.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECust2005236–237_127-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECust2005236–237-127">&#91;121&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="Bishops.27_Wars"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Bishops'_Wars">Bishops' Wars</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Bishops%27_Wars" title="Bishops&#39; Wars">Bishops' Wars</a></div>
<p>Charles perceived the unrest in Scotland as a rebellion against his authority, precipitating the <a href="/wiki/Bishops%27_Wars" title="Bishops&#39; Wars">First Bishops' War</a> in 1639.<sup id="cite_ref-128" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-128">&#91;122&#93;</a></sup> Charles did not seek subsidies from the English Parliament to wage war, but instead raised an army without parliamentary aid and marched to <a href="/wiki/Berwick-upon-Tweed" title="Berwick-upon-Tweed">Berwick-upon-Tweed</a>, on the border of Scotland.<sup id="cite_ref-129" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-129">&#91;123&#93;</a></sup> Charles's army did not engage the <a href="/wiki/Covenanter" class="mw-redirect" title="Covenanter">Covenanters</a> as the king feared the defeat of his forces, whom he believed to be significantly outnumbered by the Scots.<sup id="cite_ref-130" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-130">&#91;124&#93;</a></sup> In the <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_Berwick_(1639)" title="Treaty of Berwick (1639)">Treaty of Berwick</a>, Charles regained custody of his Scottish fortresses and secured the dissolution of the Covenanters' interim government, albeit at the decisive concession that both the Scottish Parliament and General Assembly of the Scottish Church were called.<sup id="cite_ref-131" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-131">&#91;125&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The military failure in the First Bishops' War caused a financial and diplomatic crisis for Charles that deepened when his efforts to raise funds from Spain, while simultaneously continuing his support for his Palatine relatives, led to the public humiliation of the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_the_Downs" title="Battle of the Downs">Battle of the Downs</a>, where the Dutch destroyed a Spanish bullion fleet off the coast of Kent in sight of the impotent English navy.<sup id="cite_ref-132" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-132">&#91;126&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Charles continued peace negotiations with the Scots in a bid to gain time before launching a new military campaign. Because of his financial weakness, he was forced to call Parliament into session in an attempt to raise funds for such a venture.<sup id="cite_ref-133" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-133">&#91;127&#93;</a></sup> Both English and <a href="/wiki/Parliament_of_Ireland" title="Parliament of Ireland">Irish parliaments</a> were summoned in the early months of 1640.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAdamson200711_134-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAdamson200711-134">&#91;128&#93;</a></sup> In March 1640, the Irish Parliament duly voted in a subsidy of £180,000 with the promise to raise an army 9,000 strong by the end of May.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAdamson200711_134-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAdamson200711-134">&#91;128&#93;</a></sup> In the English general election in March, however, court candidates fared badly,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELoades1974401_135-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELoades1974401-135">&#91;129&#93;</a></sup> and Charles's dealings with the English Parliament in April quickly reached stalemate.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELoades1974402_136-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELoades1974402-136">&#91;130&#93;</a></sup> The earls of <a href="/wiki/Algernon_Percy,_10th_Earl_of_Northumberland" title="Algernon Percy, 10th Earl of Northumberland">Northumberland</a> and <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Wentworth,_1st_Earl_of_Strafford" title="Thomas Wentworth, 1st Earl of Strafford">Strafford</a> attempted to broker a compromise whereby the king would agree to forfeit ship money in exchange for £650,000 (although the cost of the coming war was estimated at around £1&#160;million).<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAdamson200714_137-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAdamson200714-137">&#91;131&#93;</a></sup> Nevertheless, this alone was insufficient to produce consensus in the Commons.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAdamson200715_138-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAdamson200715-138">&#91;132&#93;</a></sup> The Parliamentarians' calls for further reforms were ignored by Charles, who still retained the support of the House of Lords. Despite the protests of Northumberland,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAdamson200717_139-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAdamson200717-139">&#91;133&#93;</a></sup> the <a href="/wiki/Short_Parliament" title="Short Parliament">Short Parliament</a> (as it came to be known) was dissolved in May 1640, less than a month after it assembled.<sup id="cite_ref-140" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-140">&#91;134&#93;</a></sup>
</p>

.trow{justify-content:center}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .tsingle{float:none!important;max-width:100%!important;box-sizing:border-box;text-align:center}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .thumbcaption{text-align:center}}</style><div class="thumb tmulti tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:303px;max-width:303px"><div class="trow"><div class="tsingle" style="width:152px;max-width:152px"><div class="thumbimage"><a href="/wiki/File:Thomas_Wentworth,_1st_Earl_of_Strafford.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d1/Thomas_Wentworth%2C_1st_Earl_of_Strafford.jpg/150px-Thomas_Wentworth%2C_1st_Earl_of_Strafford.jpg" decoding="async" width="150" height="191" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d1/Thomas_Wentworth%2C_1st_Earl_of_Strafford.jpg/225px-Thomas_Wentworth%2C_1st_Earl_of_Strafford.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d1/Thomas_Wentworth%2C_1st_Earl_of_Strafford.jpg/300px-Thomas_Wentworth%2C_1st_Earl_of_Strafford.jpg 2x" data-file-width="393" data-file-height="500" /></a></div></div><div class="tsingle" style="width:147px;max-width:147px"><div class="thumbimage"><a href="/wiki/File:William_Laud.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a8/William_Laud.jpg/145px-William_Laud.jpg" decoding="async" width="145" height="192" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a8/William_Laud.jpg/218px-William_Laud.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a8/William_Laud.jpg/290px-William_Laud.jpg 2x" data-file-width="604" data-file-height="800" /></a></div></div></div><div class="trow"><div class="thumbcaption" style="background-color:transparent">The <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Wentworth,_1st_Earl_of_Strafford" title="Thomas Wentworth, 1st Earl of Strafford">Earl of Strafford</a> (left) and <a href="/wiki/William_Laud" title="William Laud">William Laud</a> (right): two of Charles's most influential advisors during the personal rule<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKishlanskyMorrill2008_141-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKishlanskyMorrill2008-141">&#91;135&#93;</a></sup></div></div></div></div>
<p>By this stage Strafford, <a href="/wiki/Lord_Deputy_of_Ireland" title="Lord Deputy of Ireland">Lord Deputy of Ireland</a> since 1632,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGregg1981243_142-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGregg1981243-142">&#91;136&#93;</a></sup> had emerged as Charles's right-hand man and together with Laud, pursued a policy of "<a href="/wiki/Thorough" title="Thorough">Thorough</a>" that aimed to make central royal authority more efficient and effective at the expense of local or anti-government interests.<sup id="cite_ref-143" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-143">&#91;137&#93;</a></sup> Although originally a critic of the king, Strafford defected to royal service in 1628 (due in part to Buckingham's persuasion),<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEQuintrell199346_144-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEQuintrell199346-144">&#91;138&#93;</a></sup> and had since emerged, alongside Laud, as the most influential of Charles's ministers.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESharpe1992132_145-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESharpe1992132-145">&#91;139&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Bolstered by the failure of the English Short Parliament, the Scottish Parliament declared itself capable of governing without the king's consent, and in August 1640 the Covenanter army moved into the English county of <a href="/wiki/Northumberland" title="Northumberland">Northumberland</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEStevenson1973183–208_146-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEStevenson1973183–208-146">&#91;140&#93;</a></sup> Following the illness of the earl of Northumberland, who was the king's commander-in-chief, Charles and Strafford went north to command the English forces, despite Strafford being ill himself with a combination of gout and dysentery.<sup id="cite_ref-147" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-147">&#91;141&#93;</a></sup> The Scottish soldiery, many of whom were veterans of the Thirty Years' War,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEStevenson1973101_148-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEStevenson1973101-148">&#91;142&#93;</a></sup> had far greater morale and training compared to their English counterparts. They met virtually no resistance until reaching <a href="/wiki/Newcastle_upon_Tyne" title="Newcastle upon Tyne">Newcastle upon Tyne</a>, where they defeated the English forces at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Newburn" title="Battle of Newburn">Battle of Newburn</a> and occupied the city, as well as the neighbouring county of <a href="/wiki/County_Durham" title="County Durham">Durham</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-149" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-149">&#91;143&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>As demands for a parliament grew,<sup id="cite_ref-150" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-150">&#91;144&#93;</a></sup> Charles took the unusual step of summoning a <a href="/wiki/Great_council_of_peers" class="mw-redirect" title="Great council of peers">great council of peers</a>. By the time it met, on 24 September at <a href="/wiki/York" title="York">York</a>, Charles had resolved to follow the almost universal advice to call a parliament. After informing the peers that a parliament would convene in November, he asked them to consider how he could acquire funds to maintain his army against the Scots in the meantime. They recommended making peace.<sup id="cite_ref-151" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-151">&#91;145&#93;</a></sup> A cessation of arms, although not a final settlement, was negotiated in the humiliating <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_Ripon" title="Treaty of Ripon">Treaty of Ripon</a>, signed in October 1640.<sup id="cite_ref-152" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-152">&#91;146&#93;</a></sup> The treaty stated that the Scots would continue to occupy Northumberland and Durham and be paid £850 per day until peace was restored and the English Parliament recalled, which would be required to raise sufficient funds to pay the Scottish forces.<sup id="cite_ref-153" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-153">&#91;147&#93;</a></sup> Consequently, Charles summoned what later became known as the <a href="/wiki/Long_Parliament" title="Long Parliament">Long Parliament</a>. Once again, Charles's supporters fared badly at the polls. Of the 493 members of the Commons returned in November, over 350 were opposed to the king.<sup id="cite_ref-154" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-154">&#91;148&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Long_Parliament">Long Parliament</span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Long_Parliament" title="Long Parliament">Long Parliament</a></div>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Wars_of_the_Three_Kingdoms" title="Wars of the Three Kingdoms">Wars of the Three Kingdoms</a></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Tensions_escalate">Tensions escalate</span></h3>
<p>The Long Parliament proved just as difficult for Charles as had the Short Parliament. It assembled on 3 November 1640 and quickly began proceedings to impeach the king's leading counsellors of high treason.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGregg1981323_155-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGregg1981323-155">&#91;149&#93;</a></sup> Strafford was taken into custody on 10 November; Laud was impeached on 18 December; John Finch, now <a href="/wiki/Lord_Keeper_of_the_Great_Seal" title="Lord Keeper of the Great Seal">Lord Keeper of the Great Seal</a>, was impeached the following day, and he consequently fled to <a href="/wiki/The_Hague" title="The Hague">the Hague</a> with Charles's permission on 21 December.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGregg1981324–325_156-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGregg1981324–325-156">&#91;150&#93;</a></sup> To prevent the king from dissolving it at will, Parliament passed the <a href="/wiki/Triennial_Act" class="mw-redirect" title="Triennial Act">Triennial Act</a>, which required Parliament to be summoned at least once every three years, and permitted the Lord Keeper and 12 peers to summon Parliament if the king failed to do so.<sup id="cite_ref-157" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-157">&#91;151&#93;</a></sup> The Act was coupled with a subsidy bill, and so to secure the latter, Charles grudgingly granted <a href="/wiki/Royal_assent" title="Royal assent">royal assent</a> in February 1641.<sup id="cite_ref-158" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-158">&#91;152&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Strafford had become the principal target of the Parliamentarians, particularly <a href="/wiki/John_Pym" title="John Pym">John Pym</a>, and he went on trial for high treason on 22 March 1641.<sup id="cite_ref-159" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-159">&#91;153&#93;</a></sup> However, the key allegation by <a href="/wiki/Henry_Vane_the_Elder" title="Henry Vane the Elder">Sir Henry Vane</a> that Strafford had threatened to use the Irish army to subdue England was not corroborated and on 10 April Pym's case collapsed.<sup id="cite_ref-160" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-160">&#91;154&#93;</a></sup> Pym and his allies immediately launched a bill of attainder, which simply declared Strafford guilty and pronounced the sentence of death.<sup id="cite_ref-161" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-161">&#91;155&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<table role="presentation" class="mbox-small plainlinks sistersitebox" style="background-color:#f9f9f9;border:1px solid #aaa;color:#000">
<tbody><tr>
