<td class="mbox-image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4c/Wikisource-logo.svg/38px-Wikisource-logo.svg.png" decoding="async" width="38" height="40" class="noviewer" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4c/Wikisource-logo.svg/57px-Wikisource-logo.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4c/Wikisource-logo.svg/76px-Wikisource-logo.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="410" data-file-height="430" /></td>
<td class="mbox-text plainlist"><a href="/wiki/Wikisource" title="Wikisource">Wikisource</a> has original text related to this article:
<div style="margin-left: 10px;"><b><a href="https://en.wikisource.org/wiki/Speech_against_the_attainder_of_Strafford" class="extiw" title="wikisource:Speech against the attainder of Strafford">Speech against the attainder of Strafford</a></b></div></td></tr>
</tbody></table>
<p>Charles assured Strafford that "upon the word of a king you shall not suffer in life, honour or fortune",<sup id="cite_ref-162" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-162">&#91;156&#93;</a></sup> and the attainder could not succeed if Charles withheld assent.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHibbert1968154–155_163-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHibbert1968154–155-163">&#91;157&#93;</a></sup> Furthermore, many members and most peers were opposed to the attainder, not wishing, in the words of one, to "commit murder with the sword of justice".<sup id="cite_ref-164" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-164">&#91;158&#93;</a></sup> However, increased tensions and an attempted coup by royalist army officers in support of Strafford and in which Charles was involved began to sway the issue.<sup id="cite_ref-165" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-165">&#91;159&#93;</a></sup> The Commons passed the bill on 20 April by a large margin (204 in favour, 59 opposed, and 230 abstained), and the Lords acquiesced (by 26 votes to 19, with 79 absent) in May.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGregg1981329,_333_166-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGregg1981329,_333-166">&#91;160&#93;</a></sup> On 3 May, Parliament's <a href="/wiki/Protestation_of_1641" title="Protestation of 1641">Protestation</a> attacked the "wicked counsels" of Charles's "arbitrary and tyrannical government"; while those who signed the petition undertook to defend the king's "person, honour and estate", they also swore to preserve "the true reformed religion", parliament, and the "rights and liberties of the subjects".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKenyon1978127_167-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKenyon1978127-167">&#91;161&#93;</a></sup> Charles, fearing for the safety of his family in the face of unrest, assented reluctantly to Strafford's attainder on 9 May after consulting his judges and bishops.<sup id="cite_ref-168" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-168">&#91;162&#93;</a></sup> Strafford was beheaded three days later.<sup id="cite_ref-169" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-169">&#91;163&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Additionally in early May, Charles assented to an unprecedented Act that forbade the dissolution of the English Parliament without its consent.<sup id="cite_ref-170" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-170">&#91;164&#93;</a></sup> In the following months, ship money, fines in distraint of knighthood and excise without parliamentary consent were declared unlawful, and the Courts of Star Chamber and High Commission were abolished.<sup id="cite_ref-171" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-171">&#91;165&#93;</a></sup> All remaining forms of taxation were legalised and regulated by the Tonnage and Poundage Act.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKenyon1978129_172-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKenyon1978129-172">&#91;166&#93;</a></sup> The House of Commons also launched bills attacking bishops and episcopacy, but these failed in the Lords.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKenyon1978130_173-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKenyon1978130-173">&#91;167&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Charles had made important concessions in England, and temporarily improved his position in Scotland by securing the favour of the Scots on a visit from August to November 1641 during which he conceded to the official establishment of presbyterianism.<sup id="cite_ref-174" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-174">&#91;168&#93;</a></sup> However, following an attempted royalist coup in Scotland, known as "<a href="/wiki/The_Incident_(conspiracy)" title="The Incident (conspiracy)">The Incident</a>", Charles's credibility was significantly undermined.<sup id="cite_ref-175" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-175">&#91;169&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Irish_rebellion">Irish rebellion</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Irish_Rebellion_of_1641" title="Irish Rebellion of 1641">Irish Rebellion of 1641</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:King_Charles_I_by_Sir_Anthony_Van_Dyck.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d5/King_Charles_I_by_Sir_Anthony_Van_Dyck.jpg/220px-King_Charles_I_by_Sir_Anthony_Van_Dyck.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="275" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d5/King_Charles_I_by_Sir_Anthony_Van_Dyck.jpg/330px-King_Charles_I_by_Sir_Anthony_Van_Dyck.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d5/King_Charles_I_by_Sir_Anthony_Van_Dyck.jpg/440px-King_Charles_I_by_Sir_Anthony_Van_Dyck.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2400" data-file-height="3000" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:King_Charles_I_by_Sir_Anthony_Van_Dyck.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Portrait by van Dyck, c. 1635</div></div></div>
<p>In Ireland, the population was split into three main socio-political groups: the <a href="/wiki/Gaelic_Ireland" title="Gaelic Ireland">Gaelic Irish</a>, who were Catholic; the <a href="/wiki/Normans_in_Ireland" title="Normans in Ireland">Old English</a>, who were descended from <a href="/wiki/Norman_invasion_of_Ireland" title="Norman invasion of Ireland">medieval Normans</a> and were also predominantly Catholic; and the <a href="/wiki/Plantations_of_Ireland" title="Plantations of Ireland">New English</a>, who were Protestant settlers from England and Scotland aligned with the English Parliament and the Covenanters. Strafford's administration had improved the Irish economy and boosted tax revenue, but had done so by heavy-handedly imposing order.<sup id="cite_ref-176" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-176">&#91;170&#93;</a></sup> He had trained up a large Catholic army in support of the king and had weakened the authority of the Irish Parliament,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGillespie2006125_177-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGillespie2006125-177">&#91;171&#93;</a></sup> while continuing to confiscate land from Catholics for Protestant settlement at the same time as promoting a Laudian Anglicanism that was anathema to presbyterians.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECoward2003172_178-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECoward2003172-178">&#91;172&#93;</a></sup> As a result, all three groups had become disaffected.<sup id="cite_ref-179" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-179">&#91;173&#93;</a></sup> Strafford's impeachment provided a new departure for Irish politics whereby all sides joined together to present evidence against him.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGillespie2006130_180-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGillespie2006130-180">&#91;174&#93;</a></sup> In a similar manner to the English Parliament, the Old English members of the Irish Parliament argued that while opposed to Strafford they remained loyal to Charles. They argued that the king had been led astray by malign counsellors,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGillespie2006131_181-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGillespie2006131-181">&#91;175&#93;</a></sup> and that, moreover, a viceroy such as Strafford could emerge as a despotic figure instead of ensuring that the king was directly involved in governance.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGillespie2006137_182-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGillespie2006137-182">&#91;176&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Strafford's fall from power weakened Charles's influence in Ireland.<sup id="cite_ref-183" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-183">&#91;177&#93;</a></sup> The dissolution of the Irish army was unsuccessfully demanded three times by the English Commons during Strafford's imprisonment,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKenyon1978127_167-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKenyon1978127-167">&#91;161&#93;</a></sup> until Charles was eventually forced through lack of money to disband the army at the end of Strafford's trial.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERussell1991298_184-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERussell1991298-184">&#91;178&#93;</a></sup> Disputes concerning the transfer of land ownership from native Catholic to settler Protestant,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGillespie20063_185-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGillespie20063-185">&#91;179&#93;</a></sup> particularly in relation to the <a href="/wiki/Plantation_of_Ulster" title="Plantation of Ulster">plantation of Ulster</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-186" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-186">&#91;180&#93;</a></sup> coupled with resentment at moves to ensure the Irish Parliament was subordinate to the Parliament of England,<sup id="cite_ref-187" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-187">&#91;181&#93;</a></sup> sowed the seeds of rebellion. When armed conflict arose between the Gaelic Irish and New English, in late October 1641, the Old English sided with the Gaelic Irish while simultaneously professing their loyalty to the king.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESchama2001118_188-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESchama2001118-188">&#91;182&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In November 1641, the House of Commons passed the <a href="/wiki/Grand_Remonstrance" title="Grand Remonstrance">Grand Remonstrance</a>, a long list of grievances against actions by Charles's ministers committed since the beginning of his reign (that were asserted to be part of a grand Catholic conspiracy of which the king was an unwitting member),<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEStarkey2006112_189-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEStarkey2006112-189">&#91;183&#93;</a></sup> but it was in many ways a step too far by Pym and passed by only 11 votes – 159 to 148.<sup id="cite_ref-190" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-190">&#91;184&#93;</a></sup> Furthermore, the Remonstrance had very little support in the House of Lords, which the Remonstrance attacked.<sup id="cite_ref-191" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-191">&#91;185&#93;</a></sup> The tension was heightened by news of the Irish rebellion, coupled with inaccurate rumours of Charles's complicity.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELoades1974414_192-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELoades1974414-192">&#91;186&#93;</a></sup> Throughout November, a series of alarmist pamphlets published stories of atrocities in Ireland,<sup id="cite_ref-193" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-193">&#91;187&#93;</a></sup> which included massacres of New English settlers by the native Irish who could not be controlled by the Old English lords.<sup id="cite_ref-194" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-194">&#91;188&#93;</a></sup> Rumours of "papist" conspiracies circulated in England,<sup id="cite_ref-195" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-195">&#91;189&#93;</a></sup> and English anti-Catholic opinion was strengthened, damaging Charles's reputation and authority.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGregg1981341–342_196-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGregg1981341–342-196">&#91;190&#93;</a></sup>
The English Parliament distrusted Charles's motivations when he called for funds to put down the Irish rebellion; many members of the Commons suspected that forces raised by Charles might later be used against Parliament itself.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECoward2003200_197-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECoward2003200-197">&#91;191&#93;</a></sup> Pym's <a href="/wiki/Militia_Bill" class="mw-redirect" title="Militia Bill">Militia Bill</a> was intended to wrest control of the army from the king, but it did not have the support of the Lords, let alone Charles.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKenyon1978136_198-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKenyon1978136-198">&#91;192&#93;</a></sup> Instead, the Commons passed the bill as an ordinance, which they claimed did not require royal assent.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECarlton1995237_199-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECarlton1995237-199">&#91;193&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Militia_Ordinance" title="Militia Ordinance">Militia Ordinance</a> appears to have prompted more members of the Lords to support the king.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESmith1999129_200-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESmith1999129-200">&#91;194&#93;</a></sup> In an attempt to strengthen his position, Charles generated great antipathy in London, which was already fast falling into lawlessness, when he placed the <a href="/wiki/Tower_of_London" title="Tower of London">Tower of London</a> under the command of Colonel <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Lunsford" title="Thomas Lunsford">Thomas Lunsford</a>, an infamous, albeit efficient, career officer.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKenyon1978137_201-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKenyon1978137-201">&#91;195&#93;</a></sup> When rumours reached Charles that Parliament intended to impeach his wife for supposedly conspiring with the Irish rebels, the king decided to take drastic action.<sup id="cite_ref-202" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-202">&#91;196&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Five_members">Five members</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:HenriettaMariaofFrance03.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d0/HenriettaMariaofFrance03.jpg/170px-HenriettaMariaofFrance03.jpg" decoding="async" width="170" height="216" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d0/HenriettaMariaofFrance03.jpg/255px-HenriettaMariaofFrance03.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d0/HenriettaMariaofFrance03.jpg/340px-HenriettaMariaofFrance03.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1574" data-file-height="2000" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:HenriettaMariaofFrance03.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Queen <a href="/wiki/Henrietta_Maria" class="mw-redirect" title="Henrietta Maria">Henrietta Maria</a> by van Dyck, 1632</div></div></div>
<p>Charles suspected, probably correctly, that some members of the English Parliament had colluded with the invading Scots.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEStarkey2006113_203-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEStarkey2006113-203">&#91;197&#93;</a></sup> On 3 January 1642, Charles directed Parliament to give up <a href="/wiki/Five_members" class="mw-redirect" title="Five members">five members</a> of the Commons – Pym, <a href="/wiki/John_Hampden" title="John Hampden">John Hampden</a>, <a href="/wiki/Denzil_Holles,_1st_Baron_Holles" title="Denzil Holles, 1st Baron Holles">Denzil Holles</a>, <a href="/wiki/William_Strode" title="William Strode">William Strode</a> and <a href="/wiki/Arthur_Haselrig" title="Arthur Haselrig">Sir Arthur Haselrig</a> – and one peer – <a href="/wiki/Edward_Montagu,_2nd_Earl_of_Manchester" title="Edward Montagu, 2nd Earl of Manchester">Lord Mandeville</a> – on the grounds of high treason.<sup id="cite_ref-204" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-204">&#91;198&#93;</a></sup> When Parliament refused, it was possibly Henrietta Maria who persuaded Charles to arrest the five members by force, which Charles intended to carry out personally.<sup id="cite_ref-205" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-205">&#91;199&#93;</a></sup> However, news of the warrant reached Parliament ahead of him, and the wanted men slipped away by boat shortly before Charles entered the House of Commons with an armed guard on 4 January.<sup id="cite_ref-206" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-206">&#91;200&#93;</a></sup> Having displaced the Speaker, <a href="/wiki/William_Lenthall" title="William Lenthall">William Lenthall</a>, from his chair, the king asked him where the MPs had fled. Lenthall, on his knees,<sup id="cite_ref-207" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-207">&#91;201&#93;</a></sup> famously replied, "May it please your Majesty, I have neither eyes to see nor tongue to speak in this place but as the House is pleased to direct me, whose servant I am here."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERobertson200562_208-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERobertson200562-208">&#91;202&#93;</a></sup> Charles abjectly declared "all my birds have flown", and was forced to retire, empty-handed.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEStarkey2006114_209-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEStarkey2006114-209">&#91;203&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The botched arrest attempt was politically disastrous for Charles.<sup id="cite_ref-210" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-210">&#91;204&#93;</a></sup> No English sovereign had ever entered the House of Commons, and his unprecedented invasion of the chamber to arrest its members was considered a grave breach of parliamentary privilege.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGregg1981344_211-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGregg1981344-211">&#91;205&#93;</a></sup> In one stroke Charles destroyed his supporters' efforts to portray him as a defence against innovation and disorder.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELoades1974418_212-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELoades1974418-212">&#91;206&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Parliament quickly seized London, and Charles fled the capital for <a href="/wiki/Hampton_Court_Palace" title="Hampton Court Palace">Hampton Court Palace</a> on 10 January,<sup id="cite_ref-213" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-213">&#91;207&#93;</a></sup> moving two days later to <a href="/wiki/Windsor_Castle" title="Windsor Castle">Windsor Castle</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-214" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-214">&#91;208&#93;</a></sup> After sending his wife and eldest daughter to safety abroad in February, he travelled northwards, hoping to seize the military arsenal at <a href="/wiki/Kingston_upon_Hull" title="Kingston upon Hull">Hull</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-215" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-215">&#91;209&#93;</a></sup> To his dismay, he was <a href="/wiki/Siege_of_Hull_(1642)" title="Siege of Hull (1642)">rebuffed by the town's Parliamentary governor</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sir_John_Hotham,_1st_Baronet" title="Sir John Hotham, 1st Baronet">Sir John Hotham</a>, who refused him entry in April, and Charles was forced to withdraw.<sup id="cite_ref-216" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-216">&#91;210&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="English_Civil_War">English Civil War</span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/English_Civil_War" title="English Civil War">English Civil War</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Charles_Landseer_-_The_Eve_of_the_Battle_of_Edge_Hill,_1642_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7f/Charles_Landseer_-_The_Eve_of_the_Battle_of_Edge_Hill%2C_1642_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg/220px-Charles_Landseer_-_The_Eve_of_the_Battle_of_Edge_Hill%2C_1642_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="142" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7f/Charles_Landseer_-_The_Eve_of_the_Battle_of_Edge_Hill%2C_1642_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg/330px-Charles_Landseer_-_The_Eve_of_the_Battle_of_Edge_Hill%2C_1642_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7f/Charles_Landseer_-_The_Eve_of_the_Battle_of_Edge_Hill%2C_1642_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg/440px-Charles_Landseer_-_The_Eve_of_the_Battle_of_Edge_Hill%2C_1642_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2504" data-file-height="1617" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Charles_Landseer_-_The_Eve_of_the_Battle_of_Edge_Hill,_1642_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A nineteenth-century painting depicting Charles (centre in blue sash) before the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Edgehill" title="Battle of Edgehill">battle of Edgehill</a>, 1642</div></div></div>
<p>In mid-1642, both sides began to arm. Charles raised an army using the medieval method of <a href="/wiki/Commission_of_array" title="Commission of array">commission of array</a>, and Parliament called for volunteers for its militia.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECust2005350_217-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECust2005350-217">&#91;211&#93;</a></sup> Following futile negotiations, Charles raised the royal standard in <a href="/wiki/Nottingham" title="Nottingham">Nottingham</a> on 22 August 1642.<sup id="cite_ref-218" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-218">&#91;212&#93;</a></sup> At the start of the <a href="/wiki/First_English_Civil_War" title="First English Civil War">First English Civil War</a>, Charles's forces controlled roughly the Midlands, Wales, the West Country and northern England. He set up his court at <a href="/wiki/Oxford" title="Oxford">Oxford</a>. Parliament controlled London, the south-east and East Anglia, as well as the English navy.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELoades1974423–424_219-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELoades1974423–424-219">&#91;213&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>After a few skirmishes, the opposing forces met in earnest at <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Edgehill" title="Battle of Edgehill">Edgehill</a>, on 23 October 1642. Charles's nephew <a href="/wiki/Prince_Rupert_of_the_Rhine" title="Prince Rupert of the Rhine">Prince Rupert of the Rhine</a> disagreed with the battle strategy of the royalist commander <a href="/wiki/Robert_Bertie,_1st_Earl_of_Lindsey" title="Robert Bertie, 1st Earl of Lindsey">Lord Lindsey</a>, and Charles sided with Rupert. Lindsey resigned, leaving Charles to assume overall command assisted by <a href="/wiki/Patrick_Ruthven,_1st_Earl_of_Forth" title="Patrick Ruthven, 1st Earl of Forth">Lord Forth</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGregg1981366–367_220-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGregg1981366–367-220">&#91;214&#93;</a></sup> Rupert's cavalry successfully charged through the parliamentary ranks, but instead of swiftly returning to the field, rode off to plunder the parliamentary baggage train.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECarlton1995248_221-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECarlton1995248-221">&#91;215&#93;</a></sup> Lindsey, acting as a colonel, was wounded and bled to death without medical attention. The battle ended inconclusively as the daylight faded.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGregg1981368_222-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGregg1981368-222">&#91;216&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In his own words, the experience of battle had left Charles "exceedingly and deeply grieved".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECarlton1995249_223-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECarlton1995249-223">&#91;217&#93;</a></sup> He regrouped at Oxford, turning down Rupert's suggestion of an immediate attack on London. After a week, he set out for the capital on 3 November, <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Brentford_(1642)" title="Battle of Brentford (1642)">capturing Brentford</a> on the way while simultaneously continuing to negotiate with civic and parliamentary delegations. At <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Turnham_Green" title="Battle of Turnham Green">Turnham Green</a> on the outskirts of London, the royalist army met resistance from the city militia, and faced with a numerically superior force, Charles ordered a retreat.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECarlton1995249_223-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECarlton1995249-223">&#91;217&#93;</a></sup> He overwintered in Oxford, strengthening the city's defences and preparing for the next season's campaign. <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_Oxford" title="Treaty of Oxford">Peace talks</a> between the two sides collapsed in April.<sup id="cite_ref-224" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-224">&#91;218&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Wenceslas_Hollar_-_Charles_I_(State_3).jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/73/Wenceslas_Hollar_-_Charles_I_%28State_3%29.jpg/220px-Wenceslas_Hollar_-_Charles_I_%28State_3%29.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="282" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/73/Wenceslas_Hollar_-_Charles_I_%28State_3%29.jpg/330px-Wenceslas_Hollar_-_Charles_I_%28State_3%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/73/Wenceslas_Hollar_-_Charles_I_%28State_3%29.jpg/440px-Wenceslas_Hollar_-_Charles_I_%28State_3%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="4684" data-file-height="6010" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Wenceslas_Hollar_-_Charles_I_(State_3).jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Charles depicted by <a href="/wiki/Wenceslaus_Hollar" title="Wenceslaus Hollar">Wenceslaus Hollar</a> on horseback in front of his troops, 1644</div></div></div>
<p>The war continued indecisively over the next couple of years, and Henrietta Maria returned to Britain for 17 months from February 1643.<sup id="cite_ref-225" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-225">&#91;219&#93;</a></sup> After Rupert <a href="/wiki/Storming_of_Bristol" title="Storming of Bristol">captured Bristol</a> in July 1643, Charles visited the port city and laid <a href="/wiki/Siege_of_Gloucester" title="Siege of Gloucester">siege to Gloucester</a>, further up the <a href="/wiki/River_Severn" title="River Severn">river Severn</a>. His plan to undermine the city walls failed due to heavy rain, and on the approach of a parliamentary relief force, Charles lifted the siege and withdrew to <a href="/wiki/Sudeley_Castle" title="Sudeley Castle">Sudeley Castle</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECarlton1995257_226-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECarlton1995257-226">&#91;220&#93;</a></sup> The parliamentary army turned back towards London, and Charles set off in pursuit. The two armies met at <a href="/wiki/Newbury,_Berkshire" title="Newbury, Berkshire">Newbury, Berkshire</a>, on 20 September. Just as at Edgehill, the <a href="/wiki/First_Battle_of_Newbury" title="First Battle of Newbury">battle</a> stalemated at nightfall, and the armies disengaged.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECarlton1995258_227-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECarlton1995258-227">&#91;221&#93;</a></sup> In January 1644, Charles summoned a Parliament at Oxford, which was attended by about 40 peers and 118 members of the Commons; all told, the <a href="/wiki/Oxford_Parliament_(1644)" title="Oxford Parliament (1644)">Oxford Parliament</a>, which sat until March 1645, was supported by the majority of peers and about a third of the Commons.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGregg1981381–382_228-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGregg1981381–382-228">&#91;222&#93;</a></sup> Charles became disillusioned by the assembly's ineffectiveness, calling it a "mongrel" in private letters to his wife.<sup id="cite_ref-229" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-229">&#91;223&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1644, Charles remained in the southern half of England while Rupert rode north to <a href="/wiki/Relief_of_Newark" title="Relief of Newark">relieve Newark</a> and <a href="/wiki/Siege_of_York" title="Siege of York">York</a>, which were under threat from parliamentary and Scottish Covenanter armies. Charles was victorious at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Cropredy_Bridge" title="Battle of Cropredy Bridge">battle of Cropredy Bridge</a> in late June, but the royalists in the north were defeated at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Marston_Moor" title="Battle of Marston Moor">battle of Marston Moor</a> just a few days later.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGregg1981382–386_230-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGregg1981382–386-230">&#91;224&#93;</a></sup> The king continued his <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Lostwithiel" title="Battle of Lostwithiel">campaign in the south</a>, encircling and disarming the parliamentary army of the <a href="/wiki/Robert_Devereux,_3rd_Earl_of_Essex" title="Robert Devereux, 3rd Earl of Essex">Earl of Essex</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-231" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-231">&#91;225&#93;</a></sup> Returning northwards to his base at Oxford, he fought at <a href="/wiki/Second_Battle_of_Newbury" title="Second Battle of Newbury">Newbury for a second time</a> before the winter closed in; the battle ended indecisively.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGregg1981388–389_232-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGregg1981388–389-232">&#91;226&#93;</a></sup> Attempts to negotiate a settlement over the winter, while both sides re-armed and re-organised, were again unsuccessful.<sup id="cite_ref-233" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-233">&#91;227&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>At the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Naseby" title="Battle of Naseby">battle of Naseby</a> on 14 June 1645, Rupert's horsemen again mounted a successful charge against the flank of Parliament's <a href="/wiki/New_Model_Army" title="New Model Army">New Model Army</a>, but Charles's troops elsewhere on the field were pushed back by the opposing forces. Charles, attempting to rally his men, rode forward but as he did so, <a href="/wiki/Robert_Dalzell,_1st_Earl_of_Carnwath" title="Robert Dalzell, 1st Earl of Carnwath">Lord Carnwath</a> seized his bridle and pulled him back, fearing for the king's safety. Carnwath's action was misinterpreted by the royalist soldiers as a signal to move back, leading to a collapse of their position.<sup id="cite_ref-234" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-234">&#91;228&#93;</a></sup> The military balance tipped decisively in favour of Parliament.<sup id="cite_ref-235" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-235">&#91;229&#93;</a></sup> There followed a series of defeats for the royalists,<sup id="cite_ref-236" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-236">&#91;230&#93;</a></sup> and then the <a href="/wiki/Siege_of_Oxford" title="Siege of Oxford">Siege of Oxford</a>, <a href="/wiki/Charles_I%27s_journey_from_Oxford_to_the_Scottish_army_camp_near_Newark" title="Charles I&#39;s journey from Oxford to the Scottish army camp near Newark">from which Charles escaped</a> (disguised as a servant) in April 1646.<sup id="cite_ref-237" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-237">&#91;231&#93;</a></sup> He put himself into the hands of the Scottish presbyterian army besieging <a href="/wiki/Newark,_England" class="mw-redirect" title="Newark, England">Newark</a>, and was taken northwards to <a href="/wiki/Newcastle_upon_Tyne" title="Newcastle upon Tyne">Newcastle upon Tyne</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-238" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-238">&#91;232&#93;</a></sup> After nine months of negotiations, the Scots finally arrived at an agreement with the English Parliament: in exchange for £100,000, and the promise of more money in the future,<sup id="cite_ref-240" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-240">&#91;g&#93;</a></sup> the Scots withdrew from Newcastle and delivered Charles to the parliamentary commissioners in January 1647.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGregg1981411_241-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGregg1981411-241">&#91;234&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Captivity">Captivity</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Charles_I_Lami_Louvre_Inv5579.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/13/Charles_I_Lami_Louvre_Inv5579.jpg/220px-Charles_I_Lami_Louvre_Inv5579.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="164" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/13/Charles_I_Lami_Louvre_Inv5579.jpg/330px-Charles_I_Lami_Louvre_Inv5579.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/13/Charles_I_Lami_Louvre_Inv5579.jpg/440px-Charles_I_Lami_Louvre_Inv5579.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1910" data-file-height="1420" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Charles_I_Lami_Louvre_Inv5579.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Charles at Carisbrooke Castle, as painted by <a href="/wiki/Eug%C3%A8ne_Lami" title="Eugène Lami">Eugène Lami</a> in 1829</div></div></div>
<p>Parliament held Charles under house arrest at <a href="/wiki/Holdenby_House" title="Holdenby House">Holdenby House</a> in Northamptonshire until Cornet <a href="/wiki/George_Joyce" title="George Joyce">George Joyce</a> took him by threat of force from Holdenby on 3 June in the name of the New Model Army.<sup id="cite_ref-242" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-242">&#91;235&#93;</a></sup> By this time, mutual suspicion had developed between Parliament, which favoured army disbandment and presbyterianism, and the New Model Army, which was primarily officered by <a href="/wiki/Congregationalist_polity" title="Congregationalist polity">congregationalist</a>  <a href="/wiki/Independent_(religion)" title="Independent (religion)">Independents</a>, who sought a greater political role.<sup id="cite_ref-243" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-243">&#91;236&#93;</a></sup> Charles was eager to exploit the widening divisions, and apparently viewed Joyce's actions as an opportunity rather than a threat.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGregg1981412–414_244-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGregg1981412–414-244">&#91;237&#93;</a></sup> He was taken first to <a href="/wiki/Newmarket,_Suffolk" title="Newmarket, Suffolk">Newmarket</a>, at his own suggestion,<sup id="cite_ref-245" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-245">&#91;238&#93;</a></sup> and then transferred to <a href="/wiki/Oatlands_Palace" title="Oatlands Palace">Oatlands</a> and subsequently <a href="/wiki/Hampton_Court_Palace" title="Hampton Court Palace">Hampton Court</a>, while more <a href="/wiki/Heads_of_Proposals" title="Heads of Proposals">ultimately fruitless negotiations</a> took place.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECarlton1995312–314_246-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECarlton1995312–314-246">&#91;239&#93;</a></sup> By November, he determined that it would be in his best interests to escape – perhaps to France, Southern England or to <a href="/wiki/Berwick-upon-Tweed" title="Berwick-upon-Tweed">Berwick-upon-Tweed</a>, near the Scottish border.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECust2005435–436_247-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECust2005435–436-247">&#91;240&#93;</a></sup>  He fled Hampton Court on 11 November, and from the shores of <a href="/wiki/Southampton_Water" title="Southampton Water">Southampton Water</a> made contact with Colonel <a href="/wiki/Robert_Hammond_(English_army_officer)" class="mw-redirect" title="Robert Hammond (English army officer)">Robert Hammond</a>, Parliamentary Governor of the <a href="/wiki/Isle_of_Wight" title="Isle of Wight">Isle of Wight</a>, whom he apparently believed to be sympathetic.<sup id="cite_ref-248" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-248">&#91;241&#93;</a></sup> Hammond, however, confined Charles in <a href="/wiki/Carisbrooke_Castle" title="Carisbrooke Castle">Carisbrooke Castle</a> and informed Parliament that Charles was in his custody.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGregg1981419–420_249-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGregg1981419–420-249">&#91;242&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>From Carisbrooke, Charles continued to try to bargain with the various parties. In direct contrast to his previous conflict with the Scottish Kirk, on 26 December 1647 he signed a secret treaty with the Scots. Under the agreement, called the "<a href="/wiki/Engagers" title="Engagers">Engagement</a>", the Scots undertook to invade England on Charles's behalf and restore him to the throne on condition that presbyterianism be established in England for three years.<sup id="cite_ref-250" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-250">&#91;243&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The royalists rose in May 1648, igniting the <a href="/wiki/Second_English_Civil_War" title="Second English Civil War">Second Civil War</a>, and as agreed with Charles, the Scots invaded England. Uprisings in <a href="/wiki/Kent" title="Kent">Kent</a>, <a href="/wiki/Essex" title="Essex">Essex</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Cumberland" title="Cumberland">Cumberland</a>, and a rebellion in South Wales, were put down by the New Model Army, and with the defeat of the Scots at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Preston_(1648)" title="Battle of Preston (1648)">Battle of Preston</a> in August 1648, the royalists lost any chance of winning the war.<sup id="cite_ref-251" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-251">&#91;244&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Charles's only recourse was to return to negotiations,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECust2005442_252-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECust2005442-252">&#91;245&#93;</a></sup> which were held at <a href="/wiki/Newport,_Isle_of_Wight" title="Newport, Isle of Wight">Newport</a> on the Isle of Wight.<sup id="cite_ref-253" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-253">&#91;246&#93;</a></sup> On 5 December 1648, Parliament voted by 129 to 83 to continue negotiating with the king,<sup id="cite_ref-254" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-254">&#91;247&#93;</a></sup> but <a href="/wiki/Oliver_Cromwell" title="Oliver Cromwell">Oliver Cromwell</a> and the army opposed any further talks with someone they viewed as a bloody tyrant and were already taking action to consolidate their power.<sup id="cite_ref-255" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-255">&#91;248&#93;</a></sup> Hammond was replaced as Governor of the Isle of Wight on 27 November, and placed in the custody of the army the following day.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGregg1981429_256-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGregg1981429-256">&#91;249&#93;</a></sup> In <a href="/wiki/Pride%27s_Purge" title="Pride&#39;s Purge">Pride's Purge</a> on 6 and 7 December, the members of Parliament out of sympathy with the military were arrested or excluded by Colonel <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Pride" title="Thomas Pride">Thomas Pride</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-257" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-257">&#91;250&#93;</a></sup> while others stayed away voluntarily.<sup id="cite_ref-258" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-258">&#91;251&#93;</a></sup> The remaining members formed the <a href="/wiki/Rump_Parliament" title="Rump Parliament">Rump Parliament</a>. It was effectively a military coup.<sup id="cite_ref-259" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-259">&#91;252&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Trial">Trial</span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/High_Court_of_Justice_for_the_trial_of_Charles_I" title="High Court of Justice for the trial of Charles I">High Court of Justice for the trial of Charles I</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Charles_I_at_his_trial.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7f/Charles_I_at_his_trial.jpg/170px-Charles_I_at_his_trial.jpg" decoding="async" width="170" height="212" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7f/Charles_I_at_his_trial.jpg/255px-Charles_I_at_his_trial.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7f/Charles_I_at_his_trial.jpg/340px-Charles_I_at_his_trial.jpg 2x" data-file-width="482" data-file-height="600" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Charles_I_at_his_trial.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Charles at his trial, by <a href="/wiki/Edward_Bower" title="Edward Bower">Edward Bower</a>, 1649. He let his beard and hair grow long because Parliament had dismissed his barber, and he refused to let anyone else near him with a razor.<sup id="cite_ref-260" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-260">&#91;253&#93;</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>Charles was moved to <a href="/wiki/Hurst_Castle" title="Hurst Castle">Hurst Castle</a> at the end of 1648, and thereafter to <a href="/wiki/Windsor_Castle" title="Windsor Castle">Windsor Castle</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-261" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-261">&#91;254&#93;</a></sup> In January 1649, the Rump House of Commons indicted him on a charge of treason, which was rejected by the House of Lords.<sup id="cite_ref-262" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-262">&#91;255&#93;</a></sup> The idea of trying a king was a novel one.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERobertson20024–6_263-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERobertson20024–6-263">&#91;256&#93;</a></sup> The Chief Justices of the three common law courts of England – <a href="/wiki/Henry_Rolle" title="Henry Rolle">Henry Rolle</a>, <a href="/wiki/Oliver_St_John" title="Oliver St John">Oliver St John</a> and <a href="/wiki/John_Wilde_(jurist)" title="John Wilde (jurist)">John Wilde</a> – all opposed the indictment as unlawful.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEdwards199999,_109_264-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEdwards199999,_109-264">&#91;257&#93;</a></sup> The Rump Commons declared itself capable of legislating alone, passed a bill creating a separate court for Charles's trial, and declared the bill an act without the need for royal assent.<sup id="cite_ref-265" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-265">&#91;258&#93;</a></sup> The High Court of Justice established by the Act consisted of 135 commissioners, but many either refused to serve or chose to stay away.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGregg1981433_266-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGregg1981433-266">&#91;259&#93;</a></sup> Only 68 (all firm Parliamentarians) attended Charles's trial on charges of high treason and "other high crimes" that began on 20 January 1649 in <a href="/wiki/Palace_of_Westminster#Westminster_Hall" title="Palace of Westminster">Westminster Hall</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-267" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-267">&#91;260&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/John_Bradshaw_(judge)" title="John Bradshaw (judge)">John Bradshaw</a> acted as President of the Court, and the <a href="/wiki/Prosecutor" title="Prosecutor">prosecution</a> was led by the <a href="/wiki/Solicitor_General_for_England_and_Wales" title="Solicitor General for England and Wales">Solicitor General</a>, <a href="/wiki/John_Cook_(regicide)" title="John Cook (regicide)">John Cook</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-268" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-268">&#91;261&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Court-charles-I-sm.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/da/Court-charles-I-sm.jpg/170px-Court-charles-I-sm.jpg" decoding="async" width="170" height="268" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/da/Court-charles-I-sm.jpg/255px-Court-charles-I-sm.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/da/Court-charles-I-sm.jpg/340px-Court-charles-I-sm.jpg 2x" data-file-width="720" data-file-height="1134" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Court-charles-I-sm.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Charles (in the dock with his back to the viewer) facing the High Court of Justice, 1649<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGregg1981between_pages_420_and_421_269-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGregg1981between_pages_420_and_421-269">&#91;262&#93;</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>Charles was accused of treason against England by using his power to pursue his personal interest rather than the good of the country.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGardiner1906371–374_270-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGardiner1906371–374-270">&#91;263&#93;</a></sup> The charge stated that he, "for accomplishment of such his designs, and for the protecting of himself and his adherents in his and their wicked practices, to the same ends hath traitorously and maliciously levied war against the present Parliament, and the people therein represented", and that the "wicked designs, wars, and evil practices of him, the said Charles Stuart, have been, and are carried on for the advancement and upholding of a personal interest of will, power, and pretended prerogative to himself and his family, against the public interest, common right, liberty, justice, and peace of the people of this nation."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGardiner1906371–374_270-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGardiner1906371–374-270">&#91;263&#93;</a></sup> Reflecting the modern concept of <a href="/wiki/Command_responsibility" title="Command responsibility">command responsibility</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERobertson200515,_148–149_271-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERobertson200515,_148–149-271">&#91;264&#93;</a></sup> the indictment held him "guilty of all the treasons, murders, rapines, burnings, spoils, desolations, damages and mischiefs to this nation, acted and committed in the said wars, or occasioned thereby."<sup id="cite_ref-272" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-272">&#91;265&#93;</a></sup> An estimated 300,000 people, or 6% of the population, died during the war.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECarlton1995304_273-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECarlton1995304-273">&#91;266&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>
Over the first three days of the trial, whenever Charles was asked to plead, he refused,<sup id="cite_ref-274" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-274">&#91;267&#93;</a></sup> stating his objection with the words: "I would know by what power I am called hither, by what lawful authority...?"<sup id="cite_ref-275" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-275">&#91;268&#93;</a></sup> He claimed that no court had jurisdiction over a monarch,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERobertson20024–6_263-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERobertson20024–6-263">&#91;256&#93;</a></sup> that his own authority to rule had been <a href="/wiki/Divine_right_of_kings" title="Divine right of kings">given to him by God</a> and by the traditional laws of England, and that the power wielded by those trying him was only that of force of arms. Charles insisted that the trial was illegal, explaining that, <style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r886047036">.mw-parser-output .templatequote{overflow:hidden;margin:1em 0;padding:0 40px}.mw-parser-output .templatequote .templatequotecite{line-height:1.5em;text-align:left;padding-left:1.6em;margin-top:0}</style></p><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>no earthly power can justly call me (who am your King) in question as a delinquent&#160;... this day's proceeding cannot be warranted by God's laws; for, on the contrary, the authority of obedience unto Kings is clearly warranted, and strictly commanded in both the Old and New Testament&#160;... for the law of this land, I am no less confident, that no learned lawyer will affirm that an impeachment can lie against the King, they all going in his name: and one of their maxims is, that the King can do no wrong&#160;... the higher House is totally excluded; and for the House of Commons, it is too well known that the major part of them are detained or deterred from sitting&#160;... the arms I took up were only to defend the fundamental laws of this kingdom against those who have supposed my power hath totally changed the ancient government.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGardiner1906374–376_276-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGardiner1906374–376-276">&#91;269&#93;</a></sup> 
</p></blockquote><p> The court, by contrast, challenged the doctrine of <a href="/wiki/Sovereign_immunity" title="Sovereign immunity">sovereign immunity</a> and proposed that "the King of England was not a person, but an office whose every occupant was entrusted with a limited power to govern 'by and according to the laws of the land and not otherwise'."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERobertson200515_277-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERobertson200515-277">&#91;270&#93;</a></sup>
</p><table role="presentation" class="mbox-small plainlinks sistersitebox" style="background-color:#f9f9f9;border:1px solid #aaa;color:#000">
<tbody><tr>
<td class="mbox-image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4c/Wikisource-logo.svg/38px-Wikisource-logo.svg.png" decoding="async" width="38" height="40" class="noviewer" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4c/Wikisource-logo.svg/57px-Wikisource-logo.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4c/Wikisource-logo.svg/76px-Wikisource-logo.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="410" data-file-height="430" /></td>
<td class="mbox-text plainlist"><a href="/wiki/Wikisource" title="Wikisource">Wikisource</a> has original text related to this article:
<div style="margin-left: 10px;"><b><a href="https://en.wikisource.org/wiki/Death_warrant_of_King_Charles_I" class="extiw" title="wikisource:Death warrant of King Charles I">Death warrant of King Charles I</a></b></div></td></tr>
</tbody></table>
<p>At the end of the third day, Charles was removed from the court,<sup id="cite_ref-278" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-278">&#91;271&#93;</a></sup> which then heard over 30 witnesses against the king in his absence over the next two days, and on 26 January condemned him to death. The following day, the king was brought before a public session of the commission, declared guilty, and sentenced.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGregg1981440–441_279-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGregg1981440–441-279">&#91;272&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/List_of_regicides_of_Charles_I" title="List of regicides of Charles I">Fifty-nine of the commissioners</a> signed Charles's death warrant.<sup id="cite_ref-280" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-280">&#91;273&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Execution">Execution</span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Execution_of_Charles_I" title="Execution of Charles I">Execution of Charles I</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:The_execution_of_King_Charles_I_from_NPG.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9b/The_execution_of_King_Charles_I_from_NPG.jpg/220px-The_execution_of_King_Charles_I_from_NPG.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="194" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9b/The_execution_of_King_Charles_I_from_NPG.jpg/330px-The_execution_of_King_Charles_I_from_NPG.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9b/The_execution_of_King_Charles_I_from_NPG.jpg/440px-The_execution_of_King_Charles_I_from_NPG.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2400" data-file-height="2114" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:The_execution_of_King_Charles_I_from_NPG.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Contemporary German print of Charles I's beheading outside the <a href="/wiki/Banqueting_House,_Whitehall" title="Banqueting House, Whitehall">Banqueting House, Whitehall</a></div></div></div>
<p>Charles's <a href="/wiki/Decapitation" title="Decapitation">beheading</a> was scheduled for Tuesday, 30 January 1649. Two of his children remained in England under the control of the Parliamentarians: <a href="/wiki/Elizabeth_Stuart_(1635%E2%80%931650)" class="mw-redirect" title="Elizabeth Stuart (1635–1650)">Elizabeth</a> and <a href="/wiki/Henry_Stuart,_Duke_of_Gloucester" title="Henry Stuart, Duke of Gloucester">Henry</a>. They were permitted to visit him on 29 January, and he bade them a tearful farewell.<sup id="cite_ref-281" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-281">&#91;274&#93;</a></sup> The following morning, he called for two shirts to prevent the cold weather causing any noticeable shivers that the crowd could have mistaken for fear:<sup id="cite_ref-royalwebsite_282-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-royalwebsite-282">&#91;275&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-283" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-283">&#91;276&#93;</a></sup> "the season is so sharp as probably may make me shake, which some observers may imagine proceeds from fear. I would have no such imputation."<sup id="cite_ref-royalwebsite_282-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-royalwebsite-282">&#91;275&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>He walked under guard from <a href="/wiki/St_James%27s_Palace" title="St James&#39;s Palace">St James's Palace</a>, where he had been confined, to the <a href="/wiki/Palace_of_Whitehall" title="Palace of Whitehall">Palace of Whitehall</a>, where an execution scaffold had been erected in front of the <a href="/wiki/Banqueting_House,_Whitehall" title="Banqueting House, Whitehall">Banqueting House</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-284" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-284">&#91;277&#93;</a></sup> Charles was separated from spectators by large ranks of soldiers, and his last speech reached only those with him on the scaffold.<sup id="cite_ref-285" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-285">&#91;278&#93;</a></sup> He blamed his fate on his failure to prevent the execution of his loyal servant Strafford: "An unjust sentence that I suffered to take effect, is punished now by an unjust sentence on me."<sup id="cite_ref-286" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-286">&#91;279&#93;</a></sup> He declared that he had desired the liberty and freedom of the people as much as any, "but I must tell you that their liberty and freedom consists in having government&#160;... It is not their having a share in the government; that is nothing appertaining unto them. A subject and a sovereign are clean different things."<sup id="cite_ref-287" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-287">&#91;280&#93;</a></sup> He continued, "I shall go from a corruptible to an incorruptible Crown, where no disturbance can be."<sup id="cite_ref-288" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-288">&#91;281&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>At about 2:00&#160;p.m.,<sup id="cite_ref-289" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-289">&#91;282&#93;</a></sup> Charles put his head on the block after saying a prayer and signalled the executioner when he was ready by stretching out his hands; he was then beheaded with one clean stroke.<sup id="cite_ref-290" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-290">&#91;283&#93;</a></sup> According to observer <a href="/wiki/Philip_Henry_(clergyman)" class="mw-redirect" title="Philip Henry (clergyman)">Philip Henry</a>, a moan "as I never heard before and desire I may never hear again" rose from the assembled crowd,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHibbert1968280_291-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHibbert1968280-291">&#91;284&#93;</a></sup> some of whom then dipped their handkerchiefs in the king's blood as a memento.<sup id="cite_ref-292" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-292">&#91;285&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r923042769/mw-parser-output/.tmulti"/><div class="thumb tmulti tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:204px;max-width:204px"><div class="trow"><div class="tsingle" style="width:202px;max-width:202px"><div class="thumbimage"><a href="/wiki/File:DelarocheCromwell.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d6/DelarocheCromwell.jpg/200px-DelarocheCromwell.jpg" decoding="async" width="200" height="157" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d6/DelarocheCromwell.jpg/300px-DelarocheCromwell.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d6/DelarocheCromwell.jpg/400px-DelarocheCromwell.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1472" data-file-height="1158" /></a></div><div class="thumbcaption">Cromwell was said to have visited Charles's coffin, sighing "Cruel necessity!" as he did so.<sup id="cite_ref-293" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-293">&#91;286&#93;</a></sup> The story was depicted by <a href="/wiki/Paul_Delaroche" title="Paul Delaroche">Delaroche</a> in the nineteenth century.</div></div></div><div class="trow"><div class="tsingle" style="width:202px;max-width:202px"><div class="thumbimage"><a href="/wiki/File:Charles_I_Insulted_by_Cromwell%27s_Soldiers.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/70/Charles_I_Insulted_by_Cromwell%27s_Soldiers.jpg/200px-Charles_I_Insulted_by_Cromwell%27s_Soldiers.jpg" decoding="async" width="200" height="140" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/70/Charles_I_Insulted_by_Cromwell%27s_Soldiers.jpg/300px-Charles_I_Insulted_by_Cromwell%27s_Soldiers.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/70/Charles_I_Insulted_by_Cromwell%27s_Soldiers.jpg/400px-Charles_I_Insulted_by_Cromwell%27s_Soldiers.jpg 2x" data-file-width="942" data-file-height="658" /></a></div><div class="thumbcaption">Another of Delaroche's paintings, <i><a href="/wiki/Charles_I_Insulted_by_Cromwell%27s_Soldiers" title="Charles I Insulted by Cromwell&#39;s Soldiers">Charles I Insulted by Cromwell's Soldiers</a></i>, is an allegory for <a href="/wiki/Execution_of_Louis_XVI" title="Execution of Louis XVI">later events in France</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Mocking_of_Christ" class="mw-redirect" title="Mocking of Christ">mocking of Christ</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHiggins2009_294-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHiggins2009-294">&#91;287&#93;</a></sup></div></div></div></div></div>
<p>The executioner was masked and disguised, and there is debate over his identity. The commissioners approached <a href="/wiki/Richard_Brandon" title="Richard Brandon">Richard Brandon</a>, the common hangman of London, but he refused, at least at first, despite being offered £200. It is possible he relented and undertook the commission after being threatened with death, but there are others who have been named as potential candidates, including <a href="/wiki/George_Joyce" title="George Joyce">George Joyce</a>, <a href="/wiki/William_Hewlett_(regicide)" title="William Hewlett (regicide)">William Hulet</a> and <a href="/wiki/Hugh_Peters" class="mw-redirect" title="Hugh Peters">Hugh Peters</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEdwards1999173_295-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEdwards1999173-295">&#91;288&#93;</a></sup> The clean strike, confirmed by an examination of the king's body at Windsor in 1813,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERobertson2005201_296-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERobertson2005201-296">&#91;289&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-299" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-299">&#91;h&#93;</a></sup> suggests that the execution was carried out by an experienced headsman.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERobertson2005333_300-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERobertson2005333-300">&#91;292&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>It was common practice for the severed head of a traitor to be held up and exhibited to the crowd with the words "Behold the head of a traitor!"<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEdwards1999183_301-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEdwards1999183-301">&#91;293&#93;</a></sup> Although Charles's head was exhibited,<sup id="cite_ref-302" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-302">&#91;294&#93;</a></sup> the words were not used, possibly because the executioner did not want his voice recognised.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEdwards1999183_301-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEdwards1999183-301">&#91;293&#93;</a></sup> On the day after the execution, the king's head was sewn back onto his body, which was then embalmed and placed in a lead coffin.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGregg1981445_303-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGregg1981445-303">&#91;295&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The commission refused to allow Charles's burial at <a href="/wiki/Westminster_Abbey" title="Westminster Abbey">Westminster Abbey</a>, so his body was conveyed to Windsor on the night of 7 February.<sup id="cite_ref-304" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-304">&#91;296&#93;</a></sup> He was buried in private on 9 February 1649 in the Henry VIII vault in the chapel's quire, alongside the coffins of <a href="/wiki/Henry_VIII" class="mw-redirect" title="Henry VIII">Henry VIII</a> and Henry's third wife, <a href="/wiki/Jane_Seymour" title="Jane Seymour">Jane Seymour</a>, in <a href="/wiki/St_George%27s_Chapel,_Windsor_Castle" title="St George&#39;s Chapel, Windsor Castle">St George's Chapel, Windsor Castle</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-305" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-305">&#91;297&#93;</a></sup> The king's son, <a href="/wiki/Charles_II_of_England" title="Charles II of England">Charles II</a>, later planned for an elaborate royal mausoleum to be erected in <a href="/wiki/Hyde_Park,_London" title="Hyde Park, London">Hyde Park, London</a>, but it was never built.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKishlanskyMorrill2008_141-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKishlanskyMorrill2008-141">&#91;135&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Legacy">Legacy</span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/English_Interregnum" class="mw-redirect" title="English Interregnum">English Interregnum</a></div>
<p>Ten days after Charles's execution, on the day of his interment, a memoir purporting to be written by the king appeared for sale.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGregg1981445_303-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGregg1981445-303">&#91;295&#93;</a></sup> This book, the <i><a href="/wiki/Eikon_Basilike" title="Eikon Basilike">Eikon Basilike</a></i> (Greek for the "Royal Portrait"), contained an <i>apologia</i> for royal policies, and it proved an effective piece of royalist propaganda. <a href="/wiki/John_Milton" title="John Milton">John Milton</a> wrote a Parliamentary rejoinder, the <i><a href="/wiki/Eikonoklastes" title="Eikonoklastes">Eikonoklastes</a></i> ("The Iconoclast"), but the response made little headway against the pathos of the royalist book.<sup id="cite_ref-306" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-306">&#91;298&#93;</a></sup> Anglicans and royalists fashioned an image of martyrdom,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECust2005461_307-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECust2005461-307">&#91;299&#93;</a></sup> and in the <a href="/wiki/Convocations_of_Canterbury_and_York" title="Convocations of Canterbury and York">Convocations of Canterbury and York</a> of 1660 <a href="/wiki/King_Charles_the_Martyr" title="King Charles the Martyr">King Charles the Martyr</a> was added to the <a href="/wiki/Calendar_of_saints_(Church_of_England)" title="Calendar of saints (Church of England)">Church of England's liturgical calendar</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMitchell201299_308-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMitchell201299-308">&#91;300&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/High_church" title="High church">High church</a> Anglicans held special services on the anniversary of his death. Churches, such as those at <a href="/wiki/Church_of_King_Charles_the_Martyr,_Falmouth" title="Church of King Charles the Martyr, Falmouth">Falmouth</a> and <a href="/wiki/Church_of_King_Charles_the_Martyr,_Royal_Tunbridge_Wells" title="Church of King Charles the Martyr, Royal Tunbridge Wells">Tunbridge Wells</a>, and Anglican devotional societies such as the <a href="/wiki/Society_of_King_Charles_the_Martyr" title="Society of King Charles the Martyr">Society of King Charles the Martyr</a>, were founded in his honour.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKishlanskyMorrill2008_141-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKishlanskyMorrill2008-141">&#91;135&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>With the monarchy overthrown, England became a republic or "<a href="/wiki/Commonwealth_of_England" title="Commonwealth of England">Commonwealth</a>". The House of Lords was abolished by the Rump Commons, and executive power was assumed by a <a href="/wiki/English_Council_of_State" title="English Council of State">Council of State</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-309" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-309">&#91;301&#93;</a></sup> All significant military opposition in Britain and Ireland was extinguished by the forces of <a href="/wiki/Oliver_Cromwell" title="Oliver Cromwell">Oliver Cromwell</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Third_English_Civil_War" title="Third English Civil War">Third English Civil War</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Cromwellian_conquest_of_Ireland" title="Cromwellian conquest of Ireland">Cromwellian conquest of Ireland</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-310" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-310">&#91;302&#93;</a></sup> Cromwell forcibly disbanded the Rump Parliament in 1653,<sup id="cite_ref-311" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-311">&#91;303&#93;</a></sup> thereby establishing <a href="/wiki/The_Protectorate" title="The Protectorate">the Protectorate</a> with himself as <a href="/wiki/Lord_Protector" title="Lord Protector">Lord Protector</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-312" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-312">&#91;304&#93;</a></sup> Upon his death in 1658, he was briefly succeeded by his ineffective son, <a href="/wiki/Richard_Cromwell" title="Richard Cromwell">Richard</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-313" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-313">&#91;305&#93;</a></sup> Parliament was reinstated, and the monarchy was <a href="/wiki/English_Restoration" class="mw-redirect" title="English Restoration">restored</a> to Charles I's eldest son, Charles II, in 1660.<sup id="cite_ref-314" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-314">&#91;306&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Art">Art</span></h3>
<p>Partly inspired by his visit to the Spanish court in 1623,<sup id="cite_ref-315" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-315">&#91;307&#93;</a></sup> Charles became a passionate and knowledgeable art collector, amassing one of the finest art collections ever assembled.<sup id="cite_ref-316" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-316">&#91;308&#93;</a></sup> In Spain, he sat for a sketch by <a href="/wiki/Diego_Vel%C3%A1zquez" title="Diego Velázquez">Velázquez</a>, and acquired works by <a href="/wiki/Titian" title="Titian">Titian</a> and <a href="/wiki/Antonio_da_Correggio" title="Antonio da Correggio">Correggio</a>, among others.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGregg198183_317-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGregg198183-317">&#91;309&#93;</a></sup> In England, his commissions included the ceiling of the <a href="/wiki/Banqueting_House,_Whitehall" title="Banqueting House, Whitehall">Banqueting House, Whitehall</a>, by <a href="/wiki/Peter_Paul_Rubens" title="Peter Paul Rubens">Rubens</a> and paintings by other artists from the Low Countries such as <a href="/wiki/Gerard_van_Honthorst" title="Gerard van Honthorst">van Honthorst</a>, <a href="/wiki/Daniel_Mytens" class="mw-redirect" title="Daniel Mytens">Mytens</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Anthony_van_Dyck" title="Anthony van Dyck">van Dyck</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-318" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-318">&#91;310&#93;</a></sup> His close associates, including the <a href="/wiki/George_Villiers,_1st_Duke_of_Buckingham" title="George Villiers, 1st Duke of Buckingham">Duke of Buckingham</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Howard,_21st_Earl_of_Arundel" class="mw-redirect" title="Thomas Howard, 21st Earl of Arundel">Earl of Arundel</a>, shared his interest and have been dubbed the <a href="/wiki/Whitehall_Group" title="Whitehall Group">Whitehall Group</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMillar19586_319-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMillar19586-319">&#91;311&#93;</a></sup> In 1627 and 1628, Charles purchased the entire collection of the <a href="/wiki/Duke_of_Mantua" class="mw-redirect" title="Duke of Mantua">Duke of Mantua</a>, which included work by Titian, Correggio, <a href="/wiki/Raphael" title="Raphael">Raphael</a>, <a href="/wiki/Caravaggio" title="Caravaggio">Caravaggio</a>, <a href="/wiki/Andrea_del_Sarto" title="Andrea del Sarto">del Sarto</a> and <a href="/wiki/Andrea_Mantegna" title="Andrea Mantegna">Mantegna</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-320" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-320">&#91;312&#93;</a></sup> His collection grew further to encompass <a href="/wiki/Gian_Lorenzo_Bernini" title="Gian Lorenzo Bernini">Bernini</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pieter_Bruegel_the_Elder" title="Pieter Bruegel the Elder">Bruegel</a>, <a href="/wiki/Leonardo_da_Vinci" title="Leonardo da Vinci">da Vinci</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hans_Holbein_the_Younger" title="Hans Holbein the Younger">Holbein</a>, <a href="/wiki/Wenceslaus_Hollar" title="Wenceslaus Hollar">Hollar</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tintoretto" title="Tintoretto">Tintoretto</a> and <a href="/wiki/Paolo_Veronese" title="Paolo Veronese">Veronese</a>, and self-portraits by both <a href="/wiki/Albrecht_D%C3%BCrer" title="Albrecht Dürer">Dürer</a> and <a href="/wiki/Rembrandt" title="Rembrandt">Rembrandt</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGregg1981249–250,_278_321-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGregg1981249–250,_278-321">&#91;313&#93;</a></sup> By Charles's death, there were an estimated 1,760 paintings,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECarlton1995142_322-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECarlton1995142-322">&#91;314&#93;</a></sup> most of which were sold and dispersed by Parliament.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECarlton1995143_323-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECarlton1995143-323">&#91;315&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Assessments">Assessments</span></h3>
<p>In the words of <a href="/wiki/John_Philipps_Kenyon" title="John Philipps Kenyon">John Philipps Kenyon</a>, "Charles Stuart is a man of contradictions and controversy".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKenyon197893_324-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKenyon197893-324">&#91;316&#93;</a></sup> Revered by <a href="/wiki/High_Tories" class="mw-redirect" title="High Tories">high Tories</a> who considered him a saintly martyr,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKishlanskyMorrill2008_141-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKishlanskyMorrill2008-141">&#91;135&#93;</a></sup> he was condemned by <a href="/wiki/Whig_history" title="Whig history">Whig historians</a>, such as <a href="/wiki/Samuel_Rawson_Gardiner" title="Samuel Rawson Gardiner">Samuel Rawson Gardiner</a>, who thought him duplicitous and delusional.<sup id="cite_ref-325" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-325">&#91;317&#93;</a></sup> In recent decades, most historians have criticised him,<sup id="cite_ref-326" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-326">&#91;318&#93;</a></sup> the main exception being <a href="/wiki/Kevin_Sharpe_(historian)" title="Kevin Sharpe (historian)">Kevin Sharpe</a> who offered a more sympathetic view of Charles that has not been widely adopted.<sup id="cite_ref-327" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-327">&#91;319&#93;</a></sup> While Sharpe argued that the king was a dynamic man of conscience, Professor <a href="/wiki/Barry_Coward" title="Barry Coward">Barry Coward</a> thought Charles "was the most incompetent monarch of England since Henry VI",<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECoward2003xxii_328-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECoward2003xxii-328">&#91;320&#93;</a></sup> a view shared by <a href="/wiki/Ronald_Hutton" title="Ronald Hutton">Ronald Hutton</a>, who called him "the worst king we have had since the Middle Ages".<sup id="cite_ref-329" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-329">&#91;321&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Archbishop <a href="/wiki/William_Laud" title="William Laud">William Laud</a>, who was beheaded by Parliament during the war, described Charles as "A mild and gracious prince who knew not how to be, or how to be made, great."<sup id="cite_ref-330" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-330">&#91;322&#93;</a></sup> Charles was more sober and refined than his father,<sup id="cite_ref-331" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-331">&#91;323&#93;</a></sup> but he was intransigent. He deliberately pursued unpopular policies that ultimately brought ruin on himself.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECust2005466–474_332-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECust2005466–474-332">&#91;324&#93;</a></sup> Both Charles and James were advocates of the <a href="/wiki/Divine_right_of_kings" title="Divine right of kings">divine right of kings</a>, but while James's ambitions concerning <a href="/wiki/Absolutism_(European_history)" class="mw-redirect" title="Absolutism (European history)">absolute prerogative</a> were tempered by compromise and consensus with his subjects, Charles believed that he had no need to compromise or even to explain his actions.<sup id="cite_ref-333" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-333">&#91;325&#93;</a></sup> He thought he was answerable only to God. "Princes are not bound to give account of their actions," he wrote, "but to God alone".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGardiner190683_334-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGardiner190683-334">&#91;326&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span id="Titles.2C_styles.2C_honours_and_arms"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Titles,_styles,_honours_and_arms">Titles, styles, honours and arms</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Anthonis_van_Dyck_-_Equestrian_Portrait_of_Charles_I_-_National_Gallery,_London.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/98/Anthonis_van_Dyck_-_Equestrian_Portrait_of_Charles_I_-_National_Gallery%2C_London.jpg/220px-Anthonis_van_Dyck_-_Equestrian_Portrait_of_Charles_I_-_National_Gallery%2C_London.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="278" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/98/Anthonis_van_Dyck_-_Equestrian_Portrait_of_Charles_I_-_National_Gallery%2C_London.jpg/330px-Anthonis_van_Dyck_-_Equestrian_Portrait_of_Charles_I_-_National_Gallery%2C_London.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/98/Anthonis_van_Dyck_-_Equestrian_Portrait_of_Charles_I_-_National_Gallery%2C_London.jpg/440px-Anthonis_van_Dyck_-_Equestrian_Portrait_of_Charles_I_-_National_Gallery%2C_London.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3349" data-file-height="4226" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Anthonis_van_Dyck_-_Equestrian_Portrait_of_Charles_I_-_National_Gallery,_London.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Charles, as painted by <a href="/wiki/Sir_Anthony_van_Dyck" class="mw-redirect" title="Sir Anthony van Dyck">Sir Anthony van Dyck</a> between 1637 and 1638</div></div></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Titles_and_styles">Titles and styles</span></h3>
<ul><li>23 December 1600&#160;– 27 March 1625: Duke of Albany, Marquess of Ormonde, Earl of Ross and Lord Ardmannoch<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWeir1996252_335-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWeir1996252-335">&#91;327&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li>6 January 1605&#160;– 27 March 1625: Duke of York<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWeir1996252_335-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWeir1996252-335">&#91;327&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li>6 November 1612&#160;– 27 March 1625: Duke of Cornwall and Rothesay<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWeir1996252_335-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWeir1996252-335">&#91;327&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li>4 November 1616&#160;– 27 March 1625: Prince of Wales and Earl of Chester<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWeir1996252_335-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWeir1996252-335">&#91;327&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li>27 March 1625&#160;– 30 January 1649: His Majesty The King</li></ul>
<p>The official <a href="/wiki/Style_(manner_of_address)" class="mw-redirect" title="Style (manner of address)">style</a> of Charles I as king in England was "Charles, by the Grace of God, <a href="/wiki/King_of_England" class="mw-redirect" title="King of England">King of England</a>, <a href="/wiki/List_of_Scottish_monarchs" title="List of Scottish monarchs">Scotland</a>, <a href="/wiki/British_claims_to_the_French_throne" class="mw-redirect" title="British claims to the French throne">France</a> and <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Ireland" title="Kingdom of Ireland">Ireland</a>, <a href="/wiki/Defender_of_the_Faith" title="Defender of the Faith">Defender of the Faith</a>, etc."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWallis1921&#91;httpsarchiveorgstreamenglishregalyear00wallpage60mode2up_61&#93;_336-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWallis1921[httpsarchiveorgstreamenglishregalyear00wallpage60mode2up_61]-336">&#91;328&#93;</a></sup> The style "of France" was only nominal, and was used by every English monarch from <a href="/wiki/Edward_III" class="mw-redirect" title="Edward III">Edward III</a> to <a href="/wiki/George_III" class="mw-redirect" title="George III">George III</a>, regardless of the amount of French territory actually controlled.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWeir1996286_337-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWeir1996286-337">&#91;329&#93;</a></sup> The authors of his death warrant referred to him as "Charles Stuart, King of England".<sup id="cite_ref-338" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-338">&#91;330&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Honours">Honours</span></h3>
<ul><li><b>KB</b>: <a href="/wiki/Order_of_the_Bath" title="Order of the Bath">Knight of the Bath</a>, <i>6 January 1605&#160;– 27 March 1625</i><sup id="cite_ref-kt_339-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-kt-339">&#91;331&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li><b>KG</b>: <a href="/wiki/Order_of_the_Garter" title="Order of the Garter">Knight of the Garter</a>, <i>24 April 1611&#160;– 27 March 1625</i><sup id="cite_ref-kt_339-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-kt-339">&#91;331&#93;</a></sup></li></ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Arms">Arms</span></h3>
